ADP factors for existing natural
slopes in quick clay
Olga Monika Malinowska
Nordic Master in Cold Climate Engineering
Supervisor: Gustav Grimstad (NTNU)

Preface
This report represents Master’s Thesis in Geotechnical Engineering, the basis for a master’s degree
at the Norwegian University of Science and Technology (NTNU) and the Technical University of
Denmark (DTU) under the Nordic Master in Cold Climate Engineering joint programme. The
project was carried out within the Department of Civil and Environmental Engineering in NTNU and
Department of Environmental and Resource Engineering in DTU.
The duration of the project was from January to July 2024 and constituted a diploma project within
the NTNU subject TBM4900 Bygg- og miljøteknikk, masteroppgave with 30 ECTS. The topic was
supervised by Gustav Grimstad and Arnstein Watn from NTNU and Irene Rocchi from DTU.
Master’s thesis was undertaken as part of the SAUNA project (Safety of Urbanised Natural slopes/Sta-
bilitet av naturlige skråninger) led by NTNU in collaboration with Statens Vegvesen, NVE, Bane
NOR og Kvinnherad kommune.
Trondheim, 06.07.2024
Olga Monika Malinowska

II
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This report describes the results of the Master Thesis undertaken within the framework of the SAUNA
project at NTNU. The main objective regards the investigation into stability analysis of natural slopes
in quick clay, aiming to further develop the regulations concerning this topic in Norway.
The focus of this Master’s project targets the selection of ADP factors for slope stability analysis
based on laboratory studies. Norwegian clay is characterised by a high degree of anisotropy, which
implies a large variation in undrained shear strength between the active, transition and passive stress
zones in the ground. The mentioned ADP factors define the relationship between these values.
In Norway, it is common practice for undrained analysis to use the NGI-ADP soil model, whose
main input parameters are undrained shear strength and ADP factors. It is particularly suitable
for engineering applications due to the possibility of deriving parameters directly from geotechnical
investigations. The most commonly used values for ADP factors, however, are those based on the
recommendations implemented by NIFS report (2014), which are representing the results obtained
from investigations on samples from flat terrain. Due to the specific deviations of the principal
stresses from the vertical and horizontal direction in inclined terrain, it is assumed that these values
differ significantly for slopes and are considerably higher. Based on this assumption, the present
project investigates the impact on ADP factors obtanined from laboratory testing, when referenced
to the standard recommended values.
The study was based on laboratory testing for two study cases, Tofte, Halsnøy and Nardoveien
10, Trondheim. Laboratory teststing conducted on the samples included a number of index tests
as well as oedometer and triaxial tests. The report presents the procedures adopted for each test,
together with an overview of result interpretation and assessment of sample quality. In addition,
recommendations for the passive triaxial test procedure, which resulted from the author’s work on
this project, are presented.
Geotechnical testing was carried out on cylindrical samples (ϕ 54 for Tofte and ϕ 75 for Nardoveien
10). On the basis of index testing it was attempted to assess soil conditions and estimate its
mechanical properties, while the oedometer test gave an insight into the history of loading by
indicating the level of consolidation (represented by OCR value). The main focus of the investigations
was triaxial testing, which was performed as a consolidated undrained test, in isotropic or anisotropic
conditions, based on the assessed in-situ stress conditions described with K′
0. Relying on both
compressive and extensive tests, information was obtained on undrained shear strength in active and
passive conditions, and ADP parameters could be derived.
Based on the study, it was confirmed that the actual ADP values for the slopes are higher than
the values recommended by the NIFS report (2014). In order to test the impact of adopting these
parameters for the stability analysis, numerical calculations were carried out in PLAXIS using the
NGI-ADP soil model at two computational cross-sections in Tofte. Appropriate recommendations
were made on the grounds of the findings, but it was noted that the scope of the project was
insufficient to make far-reaching conclusions on this subject. It was pointed out, however, that there
may be circumstances in which more complex laboratory investigations of ADP situation in the slope
may be justified as part of the project.
III
==PAGE:6==
Sammendrag
Denne rapporten beskriver resultatene av masteroppgaven som er utført innenfor rammen av SAUNA-
prosjektet ved NTNU. Hovedmålet er å undersøke stabilitetsanalyser av naturlige skråninger i
kvikkleire, med sikte på å videreutvikle regelverket rundt dette temaet i Norge.
Dette masterprosjektet fokuserer på valg av ADP-faktorer for analyse av skråningsstabilitet basert
på laboratoriestudier. Norsk leire er karakterisert av en høy grad av anisotropi, noe som innebærer
en stor variasjon i udrenert skjærstyrke mellom de aktive, transisjons- og passive spenningssonene
i grunnen. De nevnte ADP-faktorene definerer forholdet mellom disse verdiene. I Norge er det
vanlig å bruke jordmodellen NGI-ADP for udrenert analyse, der de viktigste inngangsparameterne er
udrenert skjærfasthet og ADP-faktorer. Den er spesielt godt egnet for ingeniøranvendelser på grunn
av muligheten for å utlede parametere direkte fra geotekniske undersøkelser. De mest brukte verdiene
for ADP-faktorer er imidlertid de som er basert på anbefalingene i NIFS-rapporten (2014), som
representerer resultatene fra undersøkelser av prøver fra flatt terreng. På grunn av hovedspenningenes
spesifikke avvik fra vertikal og horisontal retning i hellende terreng, antas det at disse verdiene avviker
betydelig for skråninger og er betraktelig høyere. Med utgangspunkt i denne antakelsen undersøker
vi i dette prosjektet hvordan ADP-faktorer fra laboratorietester kan sammenlignes med resultatene
for anbefalte verdier.
Studien var basert på laboratorietesting for to case-studier, Tofte, Halsnøy og Nardoveien 10,
Trondheim.
Laboratorietestene som ble utført på prøvene omfattet en rekke rutintester samt
ødometer- og triaksialforsøk. Rapporten presenterer prosedyrene som ble brukt for hver test, sammen
med en oversikt over resultattolkning og vurdering av prøvekvaliteten. I tillegg presenteres anbefalinger
for den passive triaksiale testprosedyren, som er et resultat av forfatterens arbeid med dette prosjektet.
Geotekniske tester ble utført på sylindriske prøver (ϕ 54 for Tofte og ϕ 75 for Nardoveien 10). På
grunnlag av indekstesting ble det forsøkt å vurdere jordforholdene og estimere de mekaniske egen-
skapene, mens ødometertesten ga et innblikk i belastningshistorien ved å indikere konsolideringsnivået
(representert ved OCR-verdien). Hovedfokuset for undersøkelsene var triaksialprøving, som ble utført
som et konsolidert udrenert forsøk under isotrope eller anisotrope forhold, basert på de vurderte in
situ-spenningsforholdene beskrevet med K′
0. Ved hjelp av både trykk- og ekstensive tester ble det
innhentet informasjon om udrenert skjærstyrke under aktive og passive forhold, og ADP-parametere
kunne utledes.
Basert på undersøkelsen ble det bekreftet at de faktiske ADP-verdiene for skråningene er høyere enn
verdiene som er anbefalt i NIFS-rapporten (2014). For å teste effekten av å bruke disse parameterne
i stabilitetsanalysen ble det utført numeriske beregninger i PLAXIS med jordmodellen NGI-ADP på to
beregningstverrsnitt i Tofte. Det ble gitt anbefalinger på bakgrunn av resultatene, men det ble påpekt
at prosjektets omfang ikke var stort nok til å trekke vidtrekkende konklusjoner på dette temaet. Det
ble imidlertid påpekt at det kan være forhold som tilsier at mer komplekse laboratorieundersøkelser
av ADP-situasjonen i skråningen kan være berettiget som en del av prosjektet.

==PAGE:13==
Chapter 1
Introduction
This chapter provides an introduction to this report, describing the problem context, the research
question as well as the objectives and scope of the project. In addition, a framework plan for this
report is presented.
1.1
Background of the project
Quick clay and the associated landslides are a major challenge in northern countries such as Norway,
Sweden and Canada, hence considerable attention and resources are being devoted to the study of
this phenomenon and the development of regulations and recommendations for the investigations of
affected areas. Quick clay, i.e. sensitive clay with very low remoulded shear strength, is distinguished
by the possibility of very extensive landslides caused by seemingly minor events, such as local instability,
due to the progressive nature of the landslide. The stability of the terrain and the characteristics of
this type of clay have been the subject of extensive research, resulting in the issuing of regulation
NVE (2020), which is the basis for the practice in Norway at present.
One important characteristic of the clays found in Norway is the considerable anisotropy of material,
meaning variations in shear strength when loaded in different directions. This is a result of, amongst
other things, anisotropy of stress conditions in the soil, stress history and the effect of orientation
of mineralogy and the material’s deposition (NIFS 2014a). For stability analysis, this means that
the shear strength level varies along the sliding surface’s length depending on whether it passes
through the active, transition or passive stress zone. Experience shows that undrained shear strength
in the passive zone (suP) and transition zone (suD) is always lower than in the active zone (suA). It is
therefore important to take the soil anisotropy into account in the stability analysis, otherwise, the
resulting factor of safety may be too high.
A number of soil models have been developed to take into account the effect of clay anisotropy. One
of these is the NGI-ADP for clay in undrained conditions, widely used in Norway due to the fact
of using parameters that are directly interpretable from geotechnical investigations, namely active
undrained shear strength and ADP factors, i.e. the relationship between active and passive and
transition zone shear strength (suP/suA and suD/suA). Between 2013 and 2014, recommendations
were made regarding the adoption of the suA profile (NIFS 2014b) and ADP factors (NIFS 2014a),
which systematised the practice in Norway. In accordance with the guidelines for ADP parameters,
it was advised that laboratory results on high-quality samples be used where possible, although
the recommended values were also presented with a correlation to the soil plasticity index. For
low-plasticity clay, which includes quick clay, the recommended values are suP/suA = 0.35 and suD/suA
= 0.63.
As indicated by NIFS (2013a), these values were deduced from studies conducted on flat terrain.
The anisotropy of the clay, on the other hand, is considered to be influenced by the direction of the
1
==PAGE:14==
Chapter 1. Introduction
principal stresses, which in slopes are deflected in relation to the vertical and horizontal. It is therefore
reasonable to assume that the values presented in the recommendations are significantly lower than
the actual results for natural slopes in quick clay. It is therefore considered whether an approach in
which ADP values are derived from laboratory tests in exchange for adopting recommended values
may offer significant advantages with regard to the factor of safety in stability analyses and their
comparison with the values required by the applicable regulations.
Based on these considerations, two research questions are posed as a foundation for further studies:
• Are the ADP factors for natural slopes in quick and sensitive clay, obtained from laboratory
tests, significantly higher than the recommended values?
• How does the choice of ADP factors for natural slopes, adopted on the basis of laboratory
tests instead of the recommended value, affect the factor of safety obtained in the numerical
stability analysis?
1.2
Main objectives and scope of the thesis
Based on the mentioned issues and in response to the research questions posed, the following
objectives were identified for the project:
• Familiarisation with the current procedure for using the ADP approach to describe clay
anisotropy and its use in stability analysis.
• Learning about the procedures and interpretation of geotechnical laboratory tests (index,
oedometer and triaxial testing) and performing them on the clay samples relevant to the
project.
• Assessing the ADP factors for existing natural slopes in sensitive and quick clay based on
laboratory testing and comparing them with the currently used recommended values.
• Evaluation of the effect of the choice of ADP factors, based on recommendations or laboratory
tests, on the variation of the factor of safety obtained for the studied slope in the process
of numerical analysis. Investigating the correlation of this effect with the origin of the slope
- natural slope versus slope formed by geological processes such as previous landslide and
resulting material deposition.
The methodology of this project is based mainly on a literature review and the performance of
geotechnical laboratory tests with interpretation of the results obtained. The scope of this project
is limited to analysing the condition and studying samples from two sites - Tofte, Halsnøy and
Nardoveien 10, Trondheim, which are two of the study cases for the SAUNA project to which this
Master’s project contributes.
1.3
Structure of the report
The report for this project consists of 7 chapters and 6 appendices, which are briefly described below:
• Chapter 1 - Introduction: This chapter provides an introduction to the topic of the thesis
with a brief outline of the studied problem and the main research question. It also presents the
objectives and scope of the project as well as the structure of the report.
2
==PAGE:15==
Chapter 1. Introduction
• Chapter 2 - Theoretic background: The chapter discusses the main theoretical issues
related to the project topic through a literature review, including relevant reports and regulations.
The following topics are covered: the characteristics of quick clay, the Norwegian regulations
on areal stability analysis in soil with brittle behaviour, total stress analysis, soil anisotropy in
clay and the resulting ADP approach to modelling the undrained shear strength.
• Chapter 3 - Study cases: The chapter describes the two study cases used in this project
(Tofte, Halsnøy and Nardoveien 10, Trondheim), describing project background, location,
geotechnical conditions, available reports, previous investigations and stability analyses, which
together provide the reasoning for selecting these cases for the project.
• Chapter 4 - Sampling and laboratory soil testing: This chapter presents the procedure
for handling the sample in the laboratory and conducting selected geotechnical tests such as
index, oedometer and triaxial testing. The description is supplemented by an interpretation of
the laboratory test results and recommendations for the assessment of sample quality, all based
on the relevant literature, regulations and standards. In addition, the chapter is complemented
with recommendations for triaxial passive testing based on the author’s experience of working
on the project.
• Chapter 5 - Laboratory test results: This chapter presents the results of laboratory tests
on soil samples from the two discussed sites. It contains a description of the experiments
performed, including deviations from the procedure described in the previous chapter, and the
results together with an interpretation and discussion. The chapter merely summarises the
results, which are shown in detail in the appendices for the clarity of the report.
• Chapter 6 - Discussion: This chapter provides a discussion of the obtained results in relation
to the topic and objectives of this project. It discusses the choice of ADP parameters for the
stability analysis, followed by numerical modelling of two cross-sections in Tofte, and discusses
the implications for the Nardoveien 10 case based on results. In addition, the limitations of
this project are stated.

most important elements together with the results of the investigations and analysis. It also
provides recommendations based on the findings discussed earlier in relation to the stated
objectives.

appendices contain reports on the laboratory tests performed - index, oedometer and triaxial
testing, showing relevant plots and basic data for all tests, as well as basic interpretations
of soil parameters. Due to the significant number of tests conducted, it has been decided
to present these reports in the form of appendices, in order to make the written part of the
report more readable. However, it is emphasised that the test reports are an integral part of
this project. The presentation of the triaxial test results is based on a template developed by
Statens Vegvesen.
3
==PAGE:16==
Chapter 2
Theoretic background
This chapter provides an introduction to the subject matter of this project and addresses the theory
behind its theme. Topics covered in this section include quick clay and its properties, relevant
regulations in Norway, total stress analysis as well as undrained shear strength of clay and its
anisotropy. The overview is based on a literature review, including scientific papers, study reports
and relevant regulations.
2.1
Quick clay and its challanges
Quick clay is a soil type characterised by a brittle behaviour, meaning that it rapidly loses its strength
when overloaded. Consequently, a material with such properties can collapse abruptly without prior
warning. The material is distinguished by its progressive failure, as when one element collapses, the
force is transferred to the next element, creating a continuous failure zone. This phenomenon –
known as areal landslide (Norwegian områdeskred) – is characterised by the collapse of a large area
of land, initiated by a relatively small event. This behaviour is typical for soil materials with brittle
behaviour, with quick clay representing the extreme case (NVE 2020).
The quick clay remains today one of the biggest geotechnical issues in Norway and other northern
countries, such as Sweden, Finland, Canada, Russia and the USA (Alaska). Bjerrum (1955) mentions
some of the specific characteristics of quick clay landslides:
• There is no obvious cause for reduced stability, as ground movements are usually not observed
prior to events. This is consistent with the rapidity of failure previously mentioned.
• Landslides can occur even at very small slope inclinations.
• As a result of sliding, extreme material displacement occurs, which is associated with the
liquefaction of remoulded material.
• This type of landslide features a retrogressive nature, meaning that the initial slide triggers
further failure within the area.
• The landslide area usually broadens in the process of retrogression, so that the formation can
acquire a characteristic rounded shape with a narrow outlet into the valley.
The occurrence of quick clay in the northern regions is linked to the geological history of this area.
During the Pleistocene period, the surface of Norway and adjacent areas was subjected to the effects
of a thick ice sheet, which resulted in the removal of soil and loose rocks and the subsidence of
the land under the weight of the ice. As a result of climate warming, soil and crumbled rocks were
transported and deposited in saline seawater as the glacier retreated. The relief following the retreat
of the glacier caused the uplift of the land to such an extent that in Scandinavia it far outstripped
4
==PAGE:17==
Chapter 2. Theoretic background
the rise in sea level (even up to 200 to 220 m), and consequently, the marine deposits were elevated
above water level. At the terrain surface, this deposit was subjected to a slow flow of fresh water,
which caused leaching and significantly lowered the salinity of the pore water. This led to a reduction
in the activity of clay minerals and a decrease in liquid and plastic limits, which is responsible for the
properties of quick clay (Bjerrum (1955) and Bjerrum (1954)).
The characteristics of quick clay material are low remoulded strength and high sensitivity. The former
is manifested by the fact that, when remoulded, the material takes the form of viscous liquid and the
shear strength is close to 0. The latter is defined as a determinant of the ratio between the shear
strength of the undisturbed and remoulded material (Bjerrum (1955), Torrance (1983)). Figure 2.1
shows samples of quick clay in the undisturbed and remoulded state to illustrate the visibly different
characteristics of the material in these two conditions.
Figure 2.1: Quick clay sample in a) undisturbed and b) remoulded state.
In Torrance (1983) interpretation, the formation of quick clay is a post-depositional process driven
by several factors that influence both of the above-mentioned characteristics. In addition to the
previously discussed leaching, another important factor contributing to the formation of quick clay is
the flocculated structure characterised by a high void ratio, which allows a relatively high undisturbed
strength and the water content to be preserved. Figure 2.2 provides a simplified explanation of the
origin failure characteristics of quick clay.
Figure 2.2: Quick clay development and failure: 1) Marine clay stable flocculated structure with
saline pore water, 2) Quick clay resulting from leaching, unstable structure; 3) Collapsed structure
during an ongoing landslide, with an excess of water; 4) Remoulded clay after landslide, with denser
and more stable structure (figure reproduced from NVE (2020))
The sensitivity of clays has been the subject of many studies and various proposals have been made
for its classification, described i.a. by Skempton & Northey (1952) and Rosenqvist (1953). In current
5
==PAGE:18==
Chapter 2. Theoretic background
Norwegian practice, classification introduced by NGF (1982) is used: < 8 is low, 8 - 30 medium and
> 30 high sensitivity. In addition, the document introduces the classification of high-sensitivity clay
with remoulded shear strength < 0.5 kPa as quick clay. It is noteworthy, however, that this value
refers to the strength value obtained from the NS8015 procedure, while by following the Eurocode
the value is 0.33 kPa (Statens Vegvesen 2022a).
2.2
Regulations and practice in Norway
Currently, the main role in the regulations for the assessment of quick clay areas in Norway is played
by a document issued in 2020 by NVE, "Sikkerhet mot kvikkleireskred" (NVE 2020). It presents a
complex approach towards the problem, containing guidelines for every part of the process, describing
the identification of the landslide hazard area and their analysis.
NVE (2020) focuses on the so-called ’områdeskred’, which can be translated as areal landslide. This
type of widespread landslide in quick clay and clay with brittle behaviour can be triggered by a small
event such as a minor local instability. Areal landslide can expand to a significant size, as it can
spread outwards or backwards, as well as forwards. Particularly characteristic of quick clay is the
retrogressive type of landslide, but it can also have a rotational or planar mechanism. Given that the
extent of quick clay landslides is so large and there is no prior warning, it is essential to consider the
stability of the site when encountering this type of soil.
The first part of the area stability assessment procedure according to NVE (2020) is the determination
of the area of interest, which involves checking whether the site is located in an already registered
quick clay zone, as well as the marine boundary and the possible presence of marine deposits. For
this purpose, it is possible to use NVE’s quick clay theme map (NVE n.d.) and NGU’s map displaying
possible areas with marine clays (NGU n.d.a). In addition, geotechnical investigations performed
in Norway are collected in the public portal NADAG (NGU n.d.b), so that an assessment can be
made based on previous research in the field. If the possibility of quick clay or clay with brittle
behaviour has been identified at the site, the extent of the possible release and affected area must be
determined.
According to NVE (2020), in the case of a project within the quick clay influence area, an investigation
of the hazard zone must be undertaken. This part involves establishing the measurement category (K1
- K4), the identification of the critical slope, the field inspection and the geotechnical investigation,
based on which it has to be established whether quick clay or clay with brittle behaviour is occurring,
which would imply the necessity of checking the area stability. If the absence of such material is proven
at this stage, the procedure can be terminated. Otherwise, the next step is to establish the relevant
landslide mechanisms and their release and the affected area, hazard degree and consequences. On
this basis, an analysis of the stability of the area can be conducted, with the study of the present and
designed situations. NVE (2020) imposes the relevant requirements for the factor of safety according
to the measurement category and the hazard degree. If these are not met, appropriate measures
should be designed to improve the stability conditions as required. If, based on the analysis, a new
quick clay hazard zone has been identified or an existing one has been altered, there is an obligation
to report findings to NVE via their portal.
2.3
Total stress analysis and undrained shear strength
As presented in Skempton (1948), clay material may exhibit different structural behaviour, depending
on its level of saturation. Therefore, a stability analysis concerning clay slopes should integrate this
distinct mechanical response, while aiming at a correct implementation. A proposed methodology
consists of setting the angle of shear resistance (ϕ) to 0 for purely cohesive soils, where some specific
6
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 2. Theoretic background
conditions are met. The approach provides reliable practical applicability for civil engineering and
geotechnical problems, according to Skempton (1948), leading to a favourable estimation of the
safety factor of a slope.
Previous research has been carried out to investigate the behaviour of saturated clay in undrained
conditions, as discussed in Skempton (1948). Langtry Bell performed a series of shear box tests on
soft clay in 1915, observing that the angle of shear resistance manifests quite small amplitudes for
these conditions. Furthermore, he attempted to relate the results obtained to the active pressure,
passive pressure and bearing capacity of the soil. Fellenius pioneered the research of stability analysis,
concerning slope structures dominated by clay material, estimating a stability number correlated with
his experiments, as presented in Skempton (1948). Fellenius’ work was followed by Terzaghi, who
established the practical concept of ϕ = 0 in stability investigations, regarding saturated clays under
undrained conditions. Nevertheless, Terzaghi observed that when clay is allowed to consolidate, the
angle of shear resistance will normally be described by values greater than 0.
The analysis was followed up and confirmed by multiple research on the matter, leading to the
formulation of a failure criterion as illustrated in equation 2.1. In equation 2.1, based on the theory of
Skempton (1948), σ1 represents the major stress, σ3 signifies the minor stress, while c stands for soil
cohesion or shear strength at the specific water content of the specimen. The difference between the
major and minor stress can be classified as the compressive strength of the soil material, leading to the
possibility of expressing soil cohesion or shear strength as half of soil compressive strength. Skempton
(1948) emphasised the convenience of using such a relationship, as the compressive strength of a soil
material can be evaluated without considerable effort within laboratory experiments.
σ1 −σ3 = 2 ⋅c
(2.1)
By setting the angle of shear strength to 0 for a saturated clay in undrained conditions, a series
of graphical implications can be observed for triaxial testing, active earth pressure, passive earth
pressure, slope stability, and soil bearing capacity, as included within the work of Skempton (1948).
Active earth pressure Pa corresponding to a vertical wall can be expressed as indicated in equation 2.2,
with γz representing the density of clay, c stands for the shear strength of clay, while cw illustrates
the adhesion of clay to the wall.
Pa = γz −2 ⋅c ⋅
√
1 −cw
c
(2.2)
Passive pressure Pp can be expressed as a function dependent on the adhesion of the clay soil to the
vertical wall, as displayed in equation 2.3, 2.4, and 2.5. It should be mentioned that the coefficients
corresponding to soil’s cohesion were derived by Packshaw from the modified wedge theory developed
by Therzaghi, as described in Skempton (1948).
Pp = γz + 2 ⋅c
for
cw = 0
(2.3)
Pp = γz + 2.4 ⋅c
for
cw = 1
2 ⋅c
(2.4)
Pp = γz + 2.6 ⋅c
for
cw = c
(2.5)
Bearing capacity qu of a shallow foundation can be defined as a function of geometrical shape
describing the structure. Thereafter, the expression for a strip footing can be observed in equation
2.6, while the square footing should be evaluated according to equation 2.7. The parameters included
in both equations were described in the paragraphs above.
qu = γz + (2 + π) ⋅c
for
strip footings
(2.6)
qu = γz + 6.7 ⋅c
for
square footings
(2.7)
7
==PAGE:20==
Chapter 2. Theoretic background
Regarding slope stability, for the context of saturated clay within undrained conditions, the homoge-
neous soil situation was investigated and solved by Taylor, continuing the work initiated by Fellenius,
as presented in Skempton (1948). Hence, the factor of safety SF could be expressed as included in
equation 2.8, with R being the circle’s radius, θ is the angle of the drawn circle sector, W indicates
the depth to the bottom from the centre of gravity of the sectioned slope, while d suggests the offset
between the centre of the circle and the centre of gravity.
SF = c ⋅R2 ⋅θ
W ⋅d
(2.8)
It should be noted that the method discussed so far can encounter multiple limitations when applied
to practical problems. To begin with, the assumption that clay material behaviour is based on
ϕ=0 conditions may introduce slight deviations. It was mentioned in Skempton (1948) that even
under undrained conditions, saturated clays may have a friction angle greater than 0. Moreover, the
mechanical behaviour of clay soil type tends to be constantly controlled by true cohesins and true
friction angle during laboratory experiments. The theoretical inclination which allows shear plane
development can be considered as indicated in equation 2.9, with ϕf describing the internal friction
angle of soil material. Within the compression tests performed on multiple samples, it was found
that shear planes were propagating at angles greater than 45○, inclination specific for non-frictional
materials according to Skempton (1948). Nevertheless, the identification of shear plane inclination in
reality can pose quite a significant challenge due to the lack of homogeneity within the soil structure.
Consequently, even without precise values generated by the ϕ=0 analysis, the errors detected can be
quantified as negligible.
α = 45○+ 1
2 ⋅ϕ
(2.9)
Another limitation which can be outlined is the short-term spectrum targeted by the ϕ=0 analysis.
Even though the slow consolidation rate that characterises fully saturated clay soils under undrained
conditions may reflect the reality in many geotechnical applications (slope structural behaviour after
excavation or bearing capacity of a foundation while the upper structure is fulfilled), it should be
noted that the trapped water will be able to drain if enough time is allowed. As the consolidation
process progresses, Skempton (1948) emphasises the concern regarding the decrease of soil’s shear
strength in time, posing a consistent issue for the long-term slope stability assessment.
In addition, when the clay is not subjected to fully saturated conditions, the angle of shear strength
should not be set to zero. This aspect is particularly relevant to engineering problems such as
dam or embankment design, where the above-presented method may not be the most suitable for
implementation. Nonetheless, even with the discussed drawbacks, Skempton (1948) supports the
applicability of ϕ=0 theory for fully saturated clay soils in undrained conditions, given that the
required conditions assumed as initial conditions are met.
2.4
Anisotropy of clay and NGI-ADP model
The practical approach describing soil stability analysis in ordinary geotechnical applications usually
involves the determination of effective stresses for design purposes, as discussed in Grimstad et al.
(2012). Moreover, the undrained behaviour of the soil material appears to be treated as a form
of derivation of the effective stresses. While this methodology may be considered beneficial for
predominantly sand structures, clay formations may need to be assessed for undrained conditions to
account for effects caused by anisotropy or loading processes. Therefore, according to Grimstad et al.
(2012), a geotechnical finite element method should be implemented to increase the computational
capability for clay soils. The suggested model was named the NGI-ADP model.
8
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 2. Theoretic background
Based on Grimstad et al. (2012), the NGI-ADP model has been designed to facilitate the input
parameters usually considered in geotechnical problems involving undrained soil behaviour. One of its
key features is the ability to designate undrained shear strength as a direct property of the modelled
soil layer. Given that geotechnical applications for undrained soils typically involve low permeability
or reduced loading rates, which limit pore pressure dispersion in the medium, the NGI-ADP model
provides a valuable solution for incorporating soil column anisotropy into the computational method.
Furthermore, Grimstad et al. (2012) highlights that soft clay, which is common in Norway, can usually
be described by a high level of anisotropy. This detail affects structural parameters such as stiffness
or strength of the soil, which appear to depend on the principal plane of stress acting on the soil
formation.
The most relevant factors influencing the anisotropy of the soil can be represented as the stress
distribution in relation to the geological configuration, the constitutive particles forming the soil
structure together with their orientation and the undrained stress path generated by the applied load
(Grimstad et al. 2012). In addition, the determination of stress zones can be correlated with the
anisotropic conditions present in the soil, together with the amplitude of the applied surface forces.
The general stress distribution can be seen in Figure 2.3, which includes the active zone, the passive
zone and the transition zone (Grimstad et al. 2012).
Figure 2.3: Relating relevant laboratory tests to stress zones in the ground (figure reproduced from
Grimstad et al. (2012)
Grimstad et al. (2012) proposes a test procedure to assess both undrained strength and strain
anisotropy, by implementing separate laboratory tests on soil samples taken from the three stress
zones. Accordingly, a triaxial compression test should be carried out on a sample drilled from the
active zone, a simple shear test can be carried out on a soil sample from the transition zone, while
a triaxial extension test is recommended for the passive zone. The values obtained can then be
incorporated into the NGI-ADP model. It should be mentioned that the maximum undrained shear
strength for a soil material occurs when the force inclination reaches an amplitude of 0°, with the
formulation expressed by the equation 2.10 (Grimstad et al. 2012).
su = σ1 −σ3
2
(2.10)
Where:
σ1, σ3 - principal stresses at failure.
With the undrained shear strength being derived from effective stress analysis, soil strength is
influenced by factors such as over-consolidation ratio, soil friction angle or soil pressure coefficient at
9
==PAGE:22==
Chapter 2. Theoretic background
rest, under isotropic conditions. The NGI-ADP model was designed for practical engineering use
in terms of rapid model calibration and consideration of shear strength profiles under anisotropic
conditions (Grimstad et al. 2012). However, it has some limitations as it does not consider the effect
of shear-induced pore pressure, cyclic loading processes or the effects of varying loading rates.
2.5
Non-horizontal slopes anisotropy of clay
Soil stability models, in the plane strain calculation approach, can be assessed on the basis of active
shear strength and passive shear strength, reflecting the stress zones in the soil. Grimstad et al.
(2018) observes that the undrained shear strength determined in a triaxial compression test (active)
can show a significant difference compared to the value obtained in a triaxial tension test (passive).
Furthermore, previous studies in this area confirm that anisotropic undrained shear strength in a
passive condition tends to have a smaller amplitude than undrained shear strength in an active state.
It may therefore be particularly important to provide reliable initial data for the design model, aiming
to reproduce the slope condition as accurately as possible.
The starting point at which the initial shear stress τ0 forms and develops must be assessed on the
basis of the principal stresses acting on the soil structure. While in the case of a flat terrain the
principal stresses can be expressed in the vertical and horizontal directions, the case of a slope may
represent a more complex stress distribution in which the position of the dominant loading planes
must be determined. According to Grimstad et al. (2018), the process of geological sedimentation
can influence the direction and propagation of anisotropic layers along the soil profile. In contrast to
the NGI-ADP model, in which the initial principal stress is defined by the vertical direction and the
resting pressure coefficient K0 can be directly related to the initial shear stress τ0, the equilibrium
principle in the slope system can be influenced by the inclination, indicating the existence of a shear
stress τxy0 that is non-zero.
In order to solve the challenging geotechnical problem of an anisotropic inclined slope system, the
ADP model can be limited in its functionality, so an extended configuration has been proposed in
Grimstad et al. (2018) called ADPX. It represents a linear elastic, perfectly plastic soil model that
takes into account the effects of anisotropic soil properties. However, ADPX relies on the initial
effective stresses at the start point for its implementation.
Under isotropic conditions, the yield surface, which describes the failure thresholds for a soil element,
can be drawn based on shear stress, undrained shear strength, horizontal and vertical normal stresses.
In relation to the discussion presented in Grimstad et al. (2018), when the anisotropic effects are
considered in the analysis, the centre of the yield circle tends to move towards the initial effective
stress point, represented by the initial shear stress and the induced shear stress. It was also shown that
two dimensionless parameters ξ and η, with a range of values between 0 and 1, can be responsible
for controlling the shifting process (Grimstad et al. 2018).
The applicability of the ADPX model was investigated with a practical slope stability reconstruction
case, aiming at the Vestfossen incident of 1984 in Norway (Grimstad et al. 2018). The slope failure
occurred during the construction of an embankment along the slope profile. By incorporating the
shear-induced anisotropy effect into the stability calculation, it was found that the safety margin was
increased by 10% in relation to the isotropic phase. The increased safety margin could be translated
by an improvement of safety factor of approximately 10%. Consequently, when the principal stresses
are subjected to an initial inclination, the undrained shear strength tends to increase in contrast with
the ADP model approach (Grimstad et al. 2018).
10
==PAGE:23==
Chapter 2. Theoretic background
2.6
Recommended ADP factors
According to NIFS (2014a), ADP analysis is widely used for stability analysis in quick clay due to the
high anisotropy exhibited by low-plasticity soil. For this reason, it is necessary to take into account
the anisotropy of the soil, otherwise, it is possible to obtain results that do not correspond to the
actual situation.
In quick clay, active undrained shear strength (suA) is always higher than undrained shear strength in
passive (suP) and transition (suD) zones, with the passive state always being the lowest (figure 2.4).
It is common practice in Norway to establish the characteristic profile of undrained shear strength for
the active zone (suA) and reduce it in the transition and passive zones using so-called ADP factors
(where AP factor is suP/suA and AD factor is suD/suA).
One of the objectives of the Naturfare project (NIFS) was to develop recommendations on the
practice of applying the ADP approach for stability analysis in quick clay in Norway. This included
recommendations on the adoption of the suA profile (NIFS 2014b) and the application of ADP
factors (NIFS 2014a). As outlined in NIFS (2013b), prior to the recommendations, various ADP
factors based on experience were used extensively in practice, while data from relevant geotechnical
investigations performed at the location under consideration were used to a lesser extent.
Figure 2.4: Stress zones in the slope and corresponding shear strength conditions (reproduced from
NIFS (2014a))
NIFS (2014a) presents recommendations for the adoption of ADP factors for Norwegian clays based
on tests conducted on high-quality samples from 14 different sites in Norway. The results were
correlated with various basic soil parameters such as plasticity index, natural water content, OCR,
sensitivity and clay content. Based on the analysis of the obtained values, it was concluded that
the highest correlation could be seen between ADP parameters and the plasticity index. Figure 2.5
shows the results of the tests with recommendations for adopting ADP factors on this basis.
Accordingly, for most low plasticity Norwegian quick clays the values of the ADP factors are assumed
to be suP/suA = 0.35 and suD/suA = 0.63. However, the NIFS (2014a) indicates that the correlations
are valid for all Norwegian clays, both sensitive and non-sensitive. NIFS (2013b) and NIFS (2014a)
emphasise that the recommended values should only be used when there is no available information
for a specified location based on geotechnical testing on high-quality samples, as this gives the most
reliable and accurate data. Interestingly, NIFS (2013b) mentions that in Sweden there is an obligation
to rely on information obtained from a site-specific laboratory test when using an anisotropy approach
in design.
As part of the recommendation, an analysis of the effect of changing ADP factors on the factor of
safety obtained in the stability analysis was undertaken. The resulting change was sufficiently small.
It was noted, however, that the magnitude of the change in stability conditions is related to the
proportion of the sliding surface in the transition and passive zones, whereby the change in stability
analysis results increases with an increase in their proportion. It is also indicated that the assumed
characteristic values for the suA profile are in most cases of the greatest significance for the result
obtained.
11
==PAGE:24==
Chapter 2. Theoretic background
Figure 2.5: Recommendations regarding ADP factors on the basis of plasticity index set against
results from laboratory testing (reproduced from NIFS (2014a))
NIFS (2013a) and a discussion with the supervisors of this project, provided information on the fact
that the recommended ADP factors are based on flat ground sampling, among other things due to
the limitations of the block sampling machine, which can only take samples in a perfectly vertical
position. NIFS (2014a), as well as Grimstad et al. (2018) and Aamodt et al. (2021), addressed the
relationship between shear strength and in-situ orientation of principal stresses, indicating that ADP
relations in inclined terrain, where principal stresses deviate from vertical and horizontal directions,
differ from ones obtained in flat terrain.
12
==PAGE:25==
Chapter 3
Study cases
This chapter introduces the two study cases discussed in this project:
• stability of slope in Tofte, Halsnøy, Vestland;
• stability of slope in Nardoveien 10, Trondheim, Trøndelag.
Matters such as geographical location, geology, previous geotechnical surveys and analysis are
discussed, aiming to emphasize the relevant knowledge accessible through the existent technical
literature. Valuable lessons can be drawn in correlation with the research purpose of this thesis.
3.1
Tofte, Halsnøy
Tofte is one of the main study cases in SAUNA project. The site is located on the island Halsnøy,
Kvinnherad kommune in southern Vestland. It is an area with agricultural fields, farm buildings and
houses, situated on a slope descending towards the sea (Multiconsult 2024b).
Figure 3.1: Map (from atles.nve.no) for quick clay landslide hazard zones in Tofte, Halsnøy
An extensive survey and slope stability analysis were carried out in 2018 in connection with the planned
road widening. Based on the results, two zones of quick clay landslide hazard were established, Tofte
13
==PAGE:26==
Chapter 3. Study cases
1 and Tofte 2 - shown in figure 3.1. Subsequently, restrictions were placed on filling and groundworks
in the area (Multiconsult 2018), which have resulted in a number of analyses since then, discussed
later in this section.
Terrain and ground conditions
The area in question takes the form of a fairly even slope, descending south and south-west towards
the sea. According to the information available on the map issued by NGU online (NGU n.d.a), the
marine boundary at this location is 66 m above sea level (Multiconsult 2018). The terrain in Tofte 1
zone slopes 11-15°, while in Tofte 2 it is approximately 6-10°(Norconsult 2021a).
The site lies in a zone of marine and moraine deposits. At Tofte 1, the stratification consists of
2-4m of dry crust followed by clay or quick clay to the level of bedrock. The bedrock is located
approximately 15-25m below ground level in the upper part of the slope and approximately 5m in the
lower part. The groundwater level tends to fluctuate between 1-3 m below the ground surface. The
situation at Tofte 2 appears to be very similar, except that the rock bed can be described by a depth
of 10-15 m (Norconsult 2021a).
Important landforms for stability at this location are ravines, occurring in both Tofte 1 and Tofte 2
zones. Their origin is not clear, but it is assumed that they are the remains of an earlier landslide
or the result of erosion. In the ravine at Tofte 1, rock on the bottom is visible, while the depth
to bedrock increases on the more elevated parts of the slope profile. In addition, the ravine has a
rounded shape, which suggests that this form is the result of an earlier landslide (Multiconsult 2018).
The ravines have much steeper slopes than the terrain, with streams located at their bottom.
Significant erosion has been observed in the ravines and their immediate vicinity, and much attention
has been given to this. Therefore, the present situation will be discussed later in this section.
Ground and stability investigations
As mentioned above, an extensive ground investigation and areal stability analysis was carried out in
2018 in connection with the planned fv 544 road widening project (Statens Vegvesen (2018) and
Multiconsult (2018)). The main purpose of the expertise was to analyse the feasibility of the project,
propose possible measures to improve the stability of the area and carry out a financial analysis on
this basis.
As part of the ground investigation, a number of sounding tests were carried out and samples were
collected for laboratory testing, consisting of mini-block samples and 54mm diameter cylinders.
Laboratory testing included index testing, oedometer tests (to establish OCR and calibrate sounding
test results) and triaxial tests, both active and passive. Laboratory tests of soil from the area had
already been conducted at an earlier time, but these investigations were dealing with disturbed
material and described by a low quality. Based on the surveys from 2018, the location of the clay
with brittle behaviour was established, which is shown in figure 3.2.
On the basis of the results obtained, a stability analysis was carried out in accordance with NVE
regulations (NVE 2020), TEK 17 (DiBK 2017) and Eurocodes. The geotechnical category and
reliability category CC/CR for areal stability and local stability in materials with brittle behaviour
was assumed as 3 in accordance to Eurocode, while the measure category according to NVE (2020)
at 3 (as growth in traffic is expected).
Based on the landform, it was concluded that in addition to the areal landslide on the main slope,
a local landslide in the ravine is also possible (e.g., due to erosion or overburden), which is likely
to initiate a retrogressive landslide in the clay with brittle features. Following the NVE (2020), an
analysis of the possible landslide initiation area and its impact extent (covering the zones from the
14
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 3. Study cases
figure 3.1) was undertaken. Based on the available data, it was determined that due to the danger
and the severity of the consequences, the site has a high-risk status in connection with the quick clay
landslide hazard.
Figure 3.2: Map showing the location of the surveys conducted in 2018, designated locations outlining
the occurrence of clay with brittle behaviour (blue marking) and the location of rock outcrop (brown
marking) (reproduced from Multiconsult (2018))
On the basis of numerical stability analysis, it was detected that there might be problems with
maintaining an appropriate factor of safety for areal stability, especially on the basis of total stress
analysis. It is worth mentioning that during the interpretation of the laboratory results, ADP factors
were analyzed accordingly, which showed that the resulting values were higher than the recommended
thresholds (Figure 3.3). However, in the numerical analysis, values in line with the recommendations
were used: suD/suA = 0,63 and suP/suA = 0,35 (Multiconsult 2018).
In addition, attention was drawn to the problem of preserving ground stability in ravine areas. In
connection with the analysis carried out, it was concluded that the investment can proceed, but
with the implementation of extensive safety measures, such as filling the ravine with stones, loading
the bottom of the slope and relieving the top part. Due to the extent of the necessary measures, it
was suggested that the investment could be uneconomical, and would require resettlement and a
permanent change in the landscape. In addition, it recommended specific restrictions, along with the
need to conduct an analysis in accordance with NVE regulations, when planning future investments
made within the two quick clay landslide hazard zones. These zones have also been registered on the
NVE atlas map, as previously mentioned (Multiconsult 2018).
15
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 3. Study cases
Figure 3.3: Graph showing ADP factors derived from laboratory test results in comparison with
recommendations from NIFS rapport (NIFS 2014a). Markings "SuE @ 2%" is for shear strength read
at 2% strain while "peak SuE" is for peak shear strength values. Figure reproduced from Multiconsult
(2018).
In 2021, Multiconsult conducted a more general stability analysis (Norconsult 2021a) for both zones,
Tofte 1 and Tofte 2, based on soil surveys performed in 2018. The results obtained confirmed that
areal stability based on effective stress analysis is satisfactory, however, the conditions for total stress
analysis are not met. In addition, attention was drawn to the advancing erosion in the areas of both
ravines, especially in connection with increased water runoff from roads. It was also confirmed that
earthworks performed in the area could pose a particular factor which disturbs the areal stability. It
was also recommended that erosion protection should be implemented and that the drainage network
should be inspected and repaired if necessary.
Erosion in ravines
In recent years, there have been many reports of erosion proceeding in both ravines situated in the
area. Field inspections have been carried out, as described in reports Norconsult (2021b) and NVE
(2023). The main signs of erosion are sinkholes and cracks in the ground along the ravine. Based
on discussions with landowners and observations in the field, it is concluded that the main cause
of this state is improper drainage, which results in the leaching of fine fractions from the subsoil.
According to the report NVE (2023), erosion does not aggravate the areal stability, but NVE has
recommended regular inspections once a year and after each large rainfall. In addition, it was noted
that embankments built on the upper part of the ravine worsen the structural integrity. In figure 3.4
an example of the damage to the terrain in the ravine at Tofte 2 can be observed, due to erosional
processes.
16
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 3. Study cases
Figure 3.4: Photos of damages due to erosion in the ravine in Tofte 2 marked for the location in the
terrain (figure reproduced from Norconsult (2021b)).
Ground investigations in 2023
In 2023, a complementary soil study was ordered by NTNU as part of the SAUNA project, where
Tofte is one of the main study cases. The survey included a series of sounding tests, pore pressure
measurement in the ground, and the sampling of 16 cylinders (54mm) in 5 boreholes. The results
of the sounding and pore pressure measurement are presented in Multiconsult (2024b), while the
laboratory tests are the subject of this work and will be discussed in subsequent chapters.
3.2
Nardoveien 10, Trondheim
Nardoveien 10 is located in the Nardo district of Trondheim (figure 3.5), the south-eastern part of
the city. There are plans to demolish the existing commercial buildings on the property, followed
by the construction of student dormitories. The investor is Nardoveien 10 AS and the geotechnical
design is handled by Dr. techn. Olav Olsen AS (Olav Olsen AS 2024b).
Terrain and ground conditions
The site lies on a slope descending in a north-easterly direction. The area has undergone numerous
alterations in connection with the construction of houses and roads, creating local slopes of 1:3 to
1:20. On Nardoveien 10 site, facing the main street (Torbjørn Bratts veg), there is a commercial
17
==PAGE:30==
Chapter 3. Study cases
building with a basement, while at the back of the property a car park is built on an embankment
several metres high (Olav Olsen AS 2024b).
Figure 3.5: Location of Nardoveien 10 (reproduced from Olav Olsen AS (2024b))
There is a thick layer of marine sediments mapped in the area where the property is located, but on
the quaternary geological map, the property itself, as well as the surrounding areas, are marked as
fill material and therefore an area transformed by human activity. However, previous geotechnical
investigations have shown that underneath the fill material is a layer of marine clay, originating from
the same source as the surrounding marine sediment layer (Olav Olsen AS 2024b).
Field investigations on the property indicate that the fill mass, including the dry crust, is approximately
2 to 4 m thick, while beneath it is clay down to bedrock, which was determined to be 20 to 45m
below ground level. Based on previous experience in the area, it is assumed to be metamorphic rock
with an uniaxial compressive strength of 80-130 MPa. Above the rock level, a layer of material with
a coarse fraction was also observed in individual boreholes. Investigations show that the groundwater
level on the property and in the surrounding area generally ranges from 1-2 m in the flat part to 4 m
on the slope (Olav Olsen AS 2024b).
The clay found within the boundaries of the site is medium firm and sensitive, however, further
west up the slope a quick clay or soil with brittle behaviour has been uncovered under this layer
(Olav Olsen AS 2024b). The site is not within the quick clay hazard zone according to the map
provided by NVE (atlas.nve.no), but is located between two low-hazard zones as shown in figure 3.6.
Ground investigations
Many geotechnical investigations have been carried out on the subject site and its close neighbourhood
in recent years. In 2021, a ground investigation was conducted by Ramboll in connection with
a planned change in land use.
The survey included a range of sounding tests, pore pressure
measurements and basic laboratory tests, i.e. index testing, triaxial and oedometer tests. Based
on the survey results, a layer of clay with brittle behaviour was found at a depth of 5-14 m in the
western part of the parcel (under the car parking area). In addition, a thick layer of quick clay was
found up the slope to the west of the site (Rambøll 2021).
18
==PAGE:31==
Chapter 3. Study cases
Figure 3.6: Map (from atlas.nve.no) for quick clay landslide hazard zones near Nardoveien 10 (shown
in red) (reproduced from Olav Olsen AS (2024b))
Therefore, in the course of the geotechnical design work, it was decided to carry out additional
soil investigations on the property in question under the car park embankment, where clay with
brittle behaviour was found. Sampling was performed in mid-January 2024 by Geofield AS (figure
3.7), while laboratory tests on the sampled material were carried out by the geotechnical laboratory
Multiconsult in Trondheim. In addition, two cylinders of material from depths of 9-10 and 11-12 m
were provided to NTNU for this project.
Testing on four cylinders of material was performed in the Multiconsultu laboratory, including index
testing and two triaxial tests - one active and one passive, in undrained conditions. The triaxial tests
were conducted on soil material from a depth of 8.3 - 8.6 m, with an undrained shear strength for
the remoulded sample of 0.74 kPa and a sensitivity of around 20 (Multiconsult 2024a). Therefore, it
does not qualify as quick clay according to existing standards, but the NVE (2020) classifies it as a
material with brittle behaviour for which an analysis of areal slope stability should be investigated.
The triaxial tests were carried out for an assumed in-situ vertical stress of 105 kPa, with a K0 = 0.8
in the active zone and K0 = 1 in the passive zone (due to the technical requirements of the machine,
a small vertical force was applied during consolidation). Based on these tests, an undrained shear
strength was obtained equal to (Multiconsult 2024a):
• for active conditions: 46 kPa (for εa = 2,0%)
• for passive conditions: 22,5 kPa (for εa = -1,1%)
This means that a representative suP/suA value for this situation, based on ground investigations, is
approx. 0,49.
19
==PAGE:32==
Chapter 3. Study cases
Figure 3.7: Sampling conducted by Geofield AS on 16.01.2024 at the car parking of the property
Nardoveien 10
Stability analysis
As mentioned earlier, the geotechnical design for the project was performed by Olav Olsen AS. The
project is based on Eurocodes and Norwegian regulations, while the stability analysis is carried out
with reference to NVE (2020). The basic design assumptions include (Olav Olsen AS 2024b):
• Geotechnical Category 3 according to Eurocode 7 - as consequences of failure of a quick clay
slope are higher than for an average project, and thus the risk is also higher,
• Safety class 3 for landslides according to Norwegian technical building regulations (TEK17)
- which indicates that the maximum nominal annual probability regarding landslides cannot
exceed 1/5000 (DiBK 2017),
• Measure category 4 according to regulation NVE (2020) - which defines the factor of safety
requirements for slope stability
This means that the required factor of safety for total stress analysis is at least 1.40, while for
effective stress analysis, it is 1.2. In addition, stability conditions must not be impaired at any stage
of the project and therefore it was planned to carry out the work accordingly. According to the
assessment, the weight of the current building including the basement improves the stability of the
slope, so the filling of the area was designed in parallel with the demolition. The post-demolition
situation has also been analysed and, based on the results shown in the document, all the stability
requirements have been met (Olav Olsen AS 2024b).
In addition, due to the presence of quick clay possibly leading to large and uneven settlements as
a result, the probability of a direct foundation was discarded and tip-bearing piles founded in the
bedrock were designed. It was also recommended that the piling work be carried out cautiously and
with appropriate monitoring so that the impacts do not adversely affect the quick clay layer and thus
the areal stability (Olav Olsen AS 2024b).

cone and uniaxial tests, as well as oedometer and triaxial tests. In addition, sampling, handling of
the samples in the laboratory, along with the evaluation of sample disturbance were comprehended
within this chapter.

Chapter 4. Sampling and laboratory soil testing
The project focuses on the laboratory measurements of the mechanical properties of sensitive clay,
with particular emphasis on comparing active and passive undrained shear strength based on triaxial
tests conducted on specimens. Stability analysis for the site under consideration is an additional
element to summarise the study. In Statens Vegvesen (2022b) the parameters needed for total and
effective slope stability analysis are given, together with recommendations for suitable tests and
sources of empirical values. The mentioned overview is presented in table 4.1.
4.2
Sampling and handling samples in the laboratory
The most common type of undisturbed soil piston samples used in Norway is ∅54, but it has been
indicated that larger diameter cylinders (∅75 or ∅95) can provide samples with a considerably lower
rate of disturbance. This particularly affects the results of tests such as the triaxial and oedometer
tests, while less so the index parameter test, where it is most important to maintain the natural
water content (Helstad 2023). The piston sampling process is shown in figure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: Illustration of the piston sampling method (reproduced from NGF (1997))
However, all phases of sample handling contribute to the disturbance of the material. During
transportation to the laboratory, sample cylinders are exposed to shock and vibration, which can
be partially minimised by storing the samples in dedicated boxes. The storage method and length
also have a significant impact on sample quality. It is recommended to store the samples in cooling
chambers to preserve the in-situ temperature and to open the sample at latest 2-4 weeks after
extraction (NGF (1997) and Helstad (2023)). Amundsen (2018) recommends testing the same day
after sampling, as sample quality degrades very quickly and has a major impact on peak undrained
shear strength. Particularly decisive is the length of storage of quick clay samples, where she advises
performing the test no later than 1-2 weeks after sampling. Furthermore, she supports the idea that
storing samples for longer than a month is not advisable. In the case of material particularly prone
to disturbance, field laboratory testing should be considered.
Once a sample is opened, it is exposed to disturbance as a result of extraction, division and test
preparation. The sample is pulled out of the cylinder using a hydraulic extruder, which holds the
sample on both sides during extraction. Special care should be taken when handling the sample
afterwards in the laboratory and at resting times it should always be covered with plastic film to
prevent variations in water content. The sample is cut into specimens for testing with a wire saw.
Due to the possible disturbance of the top and bottom of the cylinder material, it is recommended
to use the middle part for tests such as triaxial or oedometer.
22
==PAGE:35==
Chapter 4. Sampling and laboratory soil testing
4.3
Index testing
Index tests are a set of measurements of basic soil properties based on national or international
standards. They are the basis for soil classification, provide information on its mechanical properties
and allow a mutual correlation (Sandven et al. 2017).
In this project, it was decided to carry out the following index tests:
• Water content
• Density measurements
• Fall cone tests
• Uniaxial tests
Other tests usually performed as part of standard procedures are Attenberg limits (plastic and liquid),
grain size distribution, salinity or organic content. In this work, it was decided to omit these tests
due to a lack of direct relevance to the topic.
4.3.1
Density
The density of the soil ρ is calculated as a relation between mass and volume (Sandven et al. 2017):
ρ = m
V = ms + mw
V

cm3]
(4.1)
where:
V - total sample volume [cm3],
(The rest of the symbols as before).
In the laboratory, density is usually measured in two ways, as an average value for the entire cylinder
and for a small sample of known volume.
A small ring, calibrated and of known mass and volume, is used to measure the density of a small
part of the specimen. It is pressed slowly into the undisturbed sample, excess material is removed
and the full ring is weighted. Afterwards, it is transferred to a container and dried in an oven at
105°C for 24 hours. On this basis, the dry density ρd is calculated:
ρd = ms
V
[ g
cm3]
(4.2)
From density measurement, the unit weight of soil γ can be derived:
γ = ρ ∗g
[kN
m3 ]
(4.3)
Generally, the unit weight for Norwegian saturated clays falls between 16 and 21 kN/m3. In addition,
grain density is often measured, but in this project, it was decided to adopt the values most
representative for these soil types in Norway, which is about 2,8 g/cm3 (Sandven et al. 2017).
4.3.2
Natural water content
The natural water content provides information on the ratio of water in the specimen to the dry
weight of the soil (Standard Norge 2014). It is determined on the small sample, min. 30 g for the
silt and clay fraction, by oven drying at 105-110°C. The sample is dried until the mass is no longer
changing, usually for 24 hours.
23
==PAGE:36==
Chapter 4. Sampling and laboratory soil testing
The natural water content w is determined by the formula (Sandven et al. 2017):
w = mw
ms
= m −ms
ms
∗100%
[%]
(4.4)
where:
m - the wet sample mass, weighed shortly after sample extraction [g],
ms - the dry sample mass, weighted after chilling [g],
mw - the mass of water within the sample [g].
Typically, water content values for Norwegian clays range between 20 and 50% and often correlate
with the soil’s mechanical properties (Sandven et al. 2017).
4.3.3
Fall cone test
The fall cone test is an easy method to estimate the undrained shear strength cu for fine-grained soil
samples. The test is conducted for both undisturbed and disturbed samples and thus the sensitivity
of the material can be determined. The method presented and used in this project is according
to Eurocode (Standard Norge 2017). However, in Norway, the methodology outlined in NS 8015
continues to be used and it gives slightly different cu results than Eurocode.
The apparatus for the fall cone test is shown in figure 4.2. It is equipped with cones of different
mass and tip angles, which can be mounted temporarily to the apparatus and afterwards released
vertically to fall freely into the sample. The apparatus with a cone can be lowered or raised and it is
equipped with a scale to measure penetration of the cone.
Figure 4.2: Fall cone apparatus in Geotechnical laboratory at NTNU
Typical cones have parameters (mass/tip angle): 10g/60°, 60g/60°, 100g/30° and 400g/30°. In
addition, a custom cone 420g/90° was made for this project. A cone type should be selected so that
it drops between 4 and 20 mm for a given sample.
24
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 4. Sampling and laboratory soil testing
The first part of the test is performed on an undisturbed sample (at least 2.5 cm in height) with
a clean and flat surface prepared. The test should be carried out at three well-spaced points on
the sample, by lowering the cone so that the tip touches the material and then releasing it. The
penetration measurement should be read immediately after the cone has fallen. No reading should
deviate from the average by more than 0,5 mm. The apparatus should then be raised with the cone
lifted vertically upward so that the sample is not further disturbed.
The second part of the examination is conducted on a remoulded sample. It is prepared with a
spatula in a cup, continuing until the consistency stops changing. It is important to avoid trapping
air bubbles inside. Any bigger elements (such as gravel or shells) should be removed and the surface
should be flattened. The procedure corresponds to the one for the undisturbed sample, whereas it is
necessary to make min. 2 measurements, then remould the sample again and repeat.
The undrained shear strength cu from the fall cone test can be assessed based on the formula
(Standard Norge 2017):
cufc or curfc = c ∗g ∗m
i2
[kPa]
(4.5)
where:
cufc - the undrained shear strength measured on the undisturbed sample,
curfc - the undrained shear strength measured on the remoulded sample,
c - constant describing the angle of the cone (c = 0,8 for 80 ° and c = 0,27 for 60 °) [−],
m - the mass of the cone [g],
i - the average cone penetration [mm].
Having both values, the sensitivity St of the soil sample can be derived:
St = cufc
curfc
[−]
(4.6)
Based on these parameters, it is possible to determine whether the clay is quick or not. According to
NS8015, quick clay is defined as having remoulded undrained shear strength cur<0,5 kPa, which
according to Eurocode will become cur<0,33 kPa. In addition, the sensitivity should reach at least
30. However, based on NVE (2020), stability analysis should be done in terms of soil with brittle
characteristics for materials with cur below 2 kPa following NS8015 and 1,27 kPa following Eurocode
(Statens Vegvesen 2022a).
4.3.4
Uniaxial test
The uniaxial test is another method for the estimation of undrained shear strength. It consists of
loading the sample axially, without deformation constraints in the horizontal direction, until failure is
reached. The strain rates applied to the sample are high, so the undrained conditions are imitated
(Sandven et al. 2017). The samples used in this study were 54 mm in diameter and 100 mm in
height. In figure 4.3 the uniaxial machine with the inspected sample are shown.
During the test, applied load P and deformations δ are registered. The initial cross-sectional area is
updated based on the strain in every step:
A = A0
1 −ϵ
[m2]
(4.7)
where:
A0 - initial sample area [m2],
ϵ - strain (deformation/initial height) [−].
25
==PAGE:38==
Chapter 4. Sampling and laboratory soil testing
Figure 4.3: Uniaxial machine in Geotechnical laboratory at NTNU
On this basis, the stresses σ are calculated:
σ = P
A
[kPa]
(4.8)
Shear stress τ is defined as half of this value, as it is acting on the plane with inclination 45° to the
measured stress (Sandven et al. 2017). The undrained shear strength is taken as the maximum value
of shear strength at failure.
Most of the clay samples of good quality undergo failure below 3% strain. A sample in which failure
occurred at a deformation of more than 5% may indicate a major disturbance of the specimen. In
figure 4.4 example load-deformation curves are shown, with shape characteristics for undisturbed and
disturbed specimens.
Mechanical parameters obtained from fall cone and uniaxial tests are not very accurate. They should
always be combined with the results of other tests, mainly triaxial and CPTU, to analyse strength
properties in the ground (Statens Vegvesen 2022a).
26
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 4. Sampling and laboratory soil testing
Figure 4.4: Example of load-deformation curves from the uniaxial machine with the curve for
undisturbed (uforstyrret) and disturbed (forstyrret) samples (reproduced from Statens Vegvesen
(2022a)).
4.4
Oedometer test
The purpose of the oedometer test is to study the deformation process in the soil and its relationship
to time and stress by measuring vertical deformations in a sample, while horizontal deformations are
constrained. In addition, it is a tool to gain an understanding of the soil’s stress history, especially
the level of pre-consolidation stresses. Therefore, it is always recommended to perform an oedometer
test before conducting a triaxial test in order to choose the right stress level in the consolidation
stage (Statens Vegvesen 2022a). Accordingly, this was also the purpose of the oedometer test in this
project, leading to the focus on this aspect in the section.
Equipment and set-up
The oedometer test equipment consists of a ring where the sample is placed and a frame for applying
the load (figure 4.5). A standard test sample in an oedometer ring has an area of 20 cm2 and a
height of 2 cm.
The sample is placed in the ring by carefully pressing it into the material, using a small amount of
silicone oil to reduce friction between the metal and soil. The excess material is then cut off with
a wire saw and removed from around the ring. The material should be placed in the oedometer
apparatus as soon as possible after the specimen is produced. Porous filters are set on both ends
of the sample. On the upper surface of the sample, drainage is enabled, while in the lower part,
there is a measuring device for pore pressure (Sandven et al. 2017). During the test, force is applied
by moving the base of the frame upward while the cap of the specimen is immobilized from above.
Within the experiment, deformation ρ, stress σ and pore pressure ub are measured.
27
==PAGE:40==
Chapter 4. Sampling and laboratory soil testing
Figure 4.5: Oedometer apparatus in Geotechnical laboratory at NTNU
Continuous loading test procedure and analysis of the results
For this project, the Constant Rate of Strain (CRS) procedure was chosen for the oedometer testing.
This procedure is more gentle on the sample and more representative of pore pressure conditions
than in the incremental loading.
The basis of this method is to achieve a constant strain rate, so the ratio dε/dt, selected with respect
to soil type, remains constant throughout the test (Sandven et al. 2017). Knowing the pore pressure
in the sample, it can be assumed that using this procedure the average effective stress σ′
m is equal to
(assuming a parabolic distribution of pore pressure at sample height):
σ′
m = σ −2
3 ∗ub
[kPa]
(4.9)
Two valuable parameters for graphically assessing pre-consolidation stress are the oedometer modulus
M and the consolidation coefficient cv. The oedometer modulus represents the stiffness of the
material under oedometer conditions and is by definition equal to:
M = dσ′
dε
[kPa]
(4.10)
On the other hand, the consolidation coefficient, which expresses the rate of soil consolidation, in
the CRS procedure can be expressed as (Sandven et al. 2017):
cv = dσ′
dt ∗[H0(1 −ε)]2
2ub
[mm2

]
(4.11)

==PAGE:41==
Chapter 4. Sampling and laboratory soil testing
To determine pre-consolidation stresses p′
c in clays, usually the results are shown in the form of 3
graphs:
• Effective stress - strain σ′ −ε
• Average effective stress - oedometer modulus σ′
m −M
• Average effective stress - coefficient of consolidation σ′
m −cv
The development of stress in the soil above the pre-consolidation stress causes a change in the grain
structure and is noticeable on all three curves: on the stress - strain graph there is a kink in the
curve, while in the corresponding graphs of modulus and the consolidation coefficient the fall of
the curve is pronounced (Statens Vegvesen 2022a). Example plots along with the interpretation of
pre-consolidation stress are shown in figure 4.6.
Figure 4.6: Example of oedometer test results - strain (tøyning)/modulus/consolidation coefficient -
stress (spenning) curves with interpretation of pre-consolidation stress p′
c (from Statens Vegvesen
(2022a))
Based on the interpreted pre-consolidation stress, it is possible to conclude the most important aspect
of soil stress history - if the material is normally consolidated (NC) or overconsolidated (OC). Normally
consolidated soil was in the past subjected only to its own weight, while in the over-consolidated
material, consolidation occurred under the influence of additional stresses that could be caused by
the presence of a glacier, erosion, landslide deposition, etc. In both cases, the magnitude of the
deformation, until p′
c is reached, is small, while later it increases significantly (Sandven et al. 2017).
