==PAGE:42==
Chapter 4. Sampling and laboratory soil testing
Sample disturbance
Figure 4.7 shows example results for good and poor quality samples.
Figure 4.7: Example of oedometer test results for a good quality sample (god prøvekvalitet) and a
poor quality sample (dårlig prøvekvalitet) (reproduced from Statens Vegvesen (2022a))
It can be seen that for poor quality samples, the M and cv values for loads prior to reaching the
pre-consolidation value are significantly lowered, while the transition of this value is not as pronounced.
Large volume strains before reaching p′
c can also be a sign of poor sample quality (Statens Vegvesen
2022a).
Decreased sample quality can have many causes, including sampling, transportation to the laboratory,
prolonged sample storage and extrusion from the cylinder. In addition, suction can occur on samples
taken from a very large depth that are subjected to sudden stress change. It can lead to considerable
disturbance if the sample is mishandled.
4.5
Triaxial test
The triaxial test is one of the methods to determine soil strength parameters and stress-strain
behaviour of soil. In-situ ground conditions are simulated by applying three-dimensional stress
conditions, controlled by cell pressure and load cell. It is also possible to adjust and monitor the pore
pressure within the sample through a back pressure system. Thus, both effective and total stresses
can be determined with respect to deformation behaviour. Another important element of the test
set-up is the ability to control drainage conditions and reproduce both active and passive ground
conditions.
The disadvantages of the triaxial test are the inability to establish a separate value for the intermediate
principal stress and the lack of possibility to change the direction of the principal stress. Several
issues, such as the effect of membrane elasticity and problems with sample and system saturation,
30
==PAGE:43==
Chapter 4. Sampling and laboratory soil testing
can directly affect the results. However, due to its fairly simple procedure and ability to simulate
representative in-situ conditions, the triaxial test remains the main laboratory method for obtaining
soil shear strength measurements.
Equipment
The main part of the triaxial machine is the cell in which the specimen is placed, allowing radial
stresses to be generated around the tested material and a frame with a load cell through which
axial stresses can be applied and measured. In addition, two water circulation systems are essential
elements: a cell- and back-pressure systems. The cell pressure system is responsible for controlling
the pressure in the triaxial cell and generates radial stresses around the sample during testing. Back
pressure, on the other hand, is responsible for saturating the sample and generating pore pressure
within it. Both devices control pressure by adding or removing water and can measure volume change
and the value of pressure. The back pressure system additionally includes a pore pressure measuring
device at the outlet of the triaxial cell. In addition, a device measuring the vertical displacement is
mounted on top of the cell, so that the change in sample height is known (Statens Vegvesen 2022a).
The triaxial machine used for this project in the Geotechnical laboratory at NTNU is shown in the
figure 4.10.
Figure 4.8: Triaxial machine (GDS) in Geotechnical laboratory at NTNU
31
==PAGE:44==
Chapter 4. Sampling and laboratory soil testing
There is one major change between the active and passive test when it comes to the load cell (figure
4.9. In the active test, the force is ensured by the load button, while in the passive test, the sample
is subjected to tension by applying suction between the load cell and the top of the sample. For this
purpose, an extension top cap with a connection to the vent valve is placed on the load cell, and a
sleeve is placed on the top cap of the sample. To create suction, the valve is closed during the shear
phase. In addition, the loading rod should be fixed to the frame in the passive test.
Figure 4.9: Load cell set-up used for: a) active triaxial test - equipped with load button; b) passive
triaxial test - with added extension top cap connected with a tube to the vent valve in the load cell,
and the sleeve which is mounted on the top of the sample.
Sample and its mounting
The triaxial machine tests cylindrical specimens - for this project, undisturbed clay samples, 54 mm
in diameter and 100 mm in height. If the cylinder from which the sample is taken has a larger
diameter, the specimen should be cut to the correct size with a wire saw before proceeding with the
test. Caution should be taken not to disturb the samples, especially if the material is very sensitive
or if there is a high content of silt.
At the bottom and on the top of the sample round stone filters are placed, previously submerged in
demineralized and de-aired water. Depending on the type of test and individual decision, paper filters
can be placed between the stone filter and the sample, as well as on its perimeter. After positioning
the sample on the pedestal, the rubber membrane is placed on the test material with the help of a
cylindrical mould. The membrane is intended to separate the sample from the water in the triaxial
chamber, allowing separate control and measurement of pore and cell pressure, therefore, it is very
important to check for any defects before use. The next step is to put the top cap on the sample
and secure the membrane at the top and bottom with O-rings. It is important to use silicon grease
between the pedestal, top cap and membrane, as it helps prevent leaks. After removing the mould,
the triaxial cell can be carefully placed and the load cell set in contact with the specimen.
When the cell is filled with water, the drainage and pore pressure system is flushed with de-aired
water. It is necessary to remove all air bubbles to correctly measure pore pressure. During flushing,
32
==PAGE:45==
Chapter 4. Sampling and laboratory soil testing
it is recommended to apply a small cell pressure (around 10 kPa) to prevent swelling of the sample.
Before starting the test, all sensors and measurements should be set to zero (Sandven et al. 2017).
Figure 4.10: Mounting the sample in the triaxial machine: a) Sample with filters on the pedestal,
b) Placing the membrane using a mould, c) Mounted sample with the top cap on, d) Triaxial cell
placed, load cell in contact with the sample.
Proposal for a test procedure
A 5-stage procedure has been adopted in this project (after a visit and discussion at the Multiconsult
Trondheim geotechnical laboratory):
1. Consolidation for in-situ conditions (using selected K’0 factor) - anisotropic or isotropic under
minimum deviatoric stresses (for keeping load cell in contact with the specimen). Both radial
and axial stresses are applied with a ramp, while pore pressure is set to 0. The stage ends
when the volume change in time is sufficiently small,
2. Saturation - back pressure ramped up to 500 kPa simultaneous with cell pressure - effective
stresses remain unchanged. Stage time - approx. 6 h,
3. B-check - cell pressure raised by 10 kPa (in one step) while undrained conditions are kept.
The change in pore pressure is assessed. Maximum time of this stage - 10 min.
4. Condition stabilisation - one-hour stage in which the effective stresses set in the consolidation
are restored (by increasing the back pressure by 10 kPa).
5. Shearing - depending on the type of the test - for the undrained test the vertical load is
increased (or decreased) by moving the base of the cylinder at a steady speed while cell pressure
is kept constant. The stage is completed when 15% of the axial strain has been reached.
Consolidation phase
Consolidation phase in the triaxial test can be performed in several different ways. In order to obtain
representative results of undrained shear strength, consolidation is carried out using an assessed
in-situ stress state in drained conditions.
Depending on the stress distribution, a distinction is made between isotropic and anisotropic
consolidation. In an isotropic system, the stresses acting on the sample are uniform, being the same
in each direction, whereas in an anisotropic state, the radial stresses differ from the vertical stresses.
These in-situ conditions are defined by the earth pressure coefficient K’0, which is equal to the ratio
33
==PAGE:46==
Chapter 4. Sampling and laboratory soil testing
of horizontal to vertical stress, usually equal to or less than 1. However, due to the equipment
constraints, in order to maintain contact between the sample and the load cell, it is recommended to
apply a small vertical load of 1 to 3 kPa also in isotropic consolidation.
In laboratory practice, there are many different ways of applying loads, depending on the equipment
available and needs. With older equipment, it was common practice to apply loads incrementally,
but nowadays it is possible to increase the stresses using a ramp. As the loading is more gentle this
way, it is therefore possible to reduce the sample disturbance.
In anisotropic loading, another aspect is how the vertical force is applied. It is common practice to
first achieve an isotropic state at horizontal stress values before applying axial load, but it is also
possible to apply a simultaneous ramp for radial stress and vertical force. According to the Statens
Vegvesen (2016), radial stresses can be applied at up to 2 kPa/min, while vertical up to 0.5 kPa/min.
In order to maintain this requirement, the deviatoric force is applied at a significantly lower rate than
the cell pressure when the anisotropic state is specified. This is also to ensure that the target K’0
coefficient level is not crossed at any time.
The consolidation phase is considered complete when, after reaching an appropriate stress state, the
volume change in time, i.e. the pore water expelled, reaches a sufficiently low level. Sandven et al.
(2017) mention, that as "a rule of thumb" the limit value can be assumed as 20mm3/5min, but
due to measurement possibilities and various disturbances, it is easiest to follow volume change in
time curve until it indicates sufficient flattening. According to Statens Vegvesen (2016), the usual
consolidation phase time for Norwegian clays is 16-24 h, however if the condition is not fulfilled
within 24 hours, the consolidation should be considered to be completed anyway. Consolidation
should be planned in such a way that at no point does sample approach failure (Helstad (2023) and
Standard Norge (2018)).
Saturation and B-test
The subsequent stage of the procedure is sample saturation, which is achieved by applying pore
pressure within the sample using a back pressure system. This stage is conducted in order to dissolve
all the air in the pores of the material to ensure accurate pore pressure measurements in the following
phases. Statens Vegvesen (2016) indicate that increasing back pressure should be done at a rate of
2-10 kPa/min and the target value between 200-500 kPa is sufficient to achieve satisfactory results
with undrained shearing. However, practice in this area varies considerably and depends on the
material being tested, for example, the tests performed on sensitive clay described in Multiconsult
(2024a) were carried out at saturation with back-pressure of 700 kPa. The saturation stage is
conducted under drained conditions, with the simultaneous raising of cell pressure in such a way that
the effective stress state set during consolidation is maintained throughout the entire phase (Helstad
2023).
After the procedure is completed, a B-test is conducted to check whether the degree of saturation
of the sample is sufficient. The test involves increasing the isotropic pressure in the chamber while
the drain is closed and observing the induced change in pore pressure in the sample. Typically, the
change in cell pressure is assumed as 10 kPa, but it is acceptable to use a value of up to 100 kPa if
necessary (for example, for overconsolidated soil material). The pore pressure response is measured
by the B-value, which is defined as 4.12:
B = ∆u
∆σc
[−]
(4.12)
where:
∆u - observed change in pore pressure [kPa],
∆σc - imposed change in cell pressure [kPa].
34
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 4. Sampling and laboratory soil testing
The phase can last from 2 to 10 min.
Statens Vegvesen (2016) indicates that the minimum
satisfactory value is B=0.95. If this condition is not met, the saturation phase should be repeated
by increasing the back pressure or lengthening the saturation time, and another B test should be
performed. However, if the B-value is still below 0.95 after 2 tests, and the two measurements do not
differ significantly, it can proceed to the shear phase without meeting this condition (Helstad 2023).
Before proceeding to the final stage, it is necessary to stabilize the test conditions by returning to
the effective stresses established in the consolidation for about 1 h (Statens Vegvesen 2016).
Shearing phase
This project focuses on finding parameters for undrained stability analysis, therefore the attention
was set on consolidated undrained triaxial tests (CIU and CAU). It means that in the shearing phase,
the drainage of the sample is closed while the sample is loaded, which enables pore pressure building
but prevents volume change. Cell pressure is maintained at a constant level during this stage, while
axial force is applied at a fixed strain-controlled rate (Helstad 2023). Tests for drained condition
differ from the presented procedure in having the draining system open, both on the top and bottom
of the sample, allowing water to flow in and out freely, and making sure that pore pressure does not
build up during vertical force application.
Statens Vegvesen (2016) recommend a rate of 2%/hour for compression (active) tests and 1.5%/hour
for extension (passive) tests. These tests differ in the direction of force application and, as mentioned
earlier, have a slightly different set-up, which in the case of the passive test makes it possible to
apply tension loading of the sample using suction between the load cell and the top cap.
The shearing phase continues until the failure criterion is reached, which is usually set at 10-15%
(Statens Vegvesen 2016). Based on stress and strain measurements obtained from this phase, further
interpretation of soil behaviour, response and parameter analysis for soil modelling is possible.
Interpretation of results
Throughout the test, the change in a set of parameters over time is measured in the triaxial machine.
Some parameters are related to the geometry of the sample, such as deformation (∆H) and change
in volume (∆V ) (based on water expulsion), others refer to the applied loads - cell pressure (σc),
back pressure (σb), pore pressure (u) and axial force (P). On this basis, a record of the stress state
and response of the material is obtained.
Based on the observed values of the interrogated parameters, it is possible to calculate the current
state of stress and update the geometry of the sample, which changes with the loading. The methods
of calculating the requested parameters are standardised in Standard Norge (2018).
In the GDS machine used in NTNU laboratory, the deformations of the sample and its volumetric
change could be read directly by the corresponding transducers. Based on these readings, the height
of the sample (eq. 4.13) and its cross-sectional area (eq. 4.14) can be updated.
Hcor = H0 −∆H
[mm]
(4.13)
Acor = V0 −∆V
H0 −∆H
[mm2]
(4.14)
Vertical (eq. 4.15) and horizontal (eq. 4.16) stresses can be calculated based on cell pressure and
vertical force.
σv = P + σc ∗Acor

[kPa]
(4.15)

to formulas 4.17 or 4.18.
For εsv ≤2% ∶(σv)fp = εsv ∗Kfp ∗Pfp

The results of the shearing phase are usually presented in the form of stress paths. In figure 4.11 the
three most commonly used interpretations are shown.
The NTH plot, commonly used in Statens Vegvesen, represents shear stress in relation to effective
radial stress, however, it is not suitable for passive tests. The MIT representation is more universal
and can compile both active and passive test results. This plot shows the effective normal stress set
against maximum shear stress (Statens Vegvesen 2022a).
However, one of the most commonly used representations is q-p’, combining deviatoric stress (eq.
4.24) and mean effective stress (eq. 4.25).
q = 0.5 ∗(σ1 + σ2 + σ3) [kPa]
(4.24)
p′ = σ1 −σ3 [kPa]
(4.25)

Figure 4.11: Most commonly used stress paths: NTH, MIT and q-p’ (figures reproduced from Statens
Vegvesen (2022a).
The two axes represent volumetric and shear effects. It allows to see clearly the dilatancy in the
mobilisation phase. However, the downside of this type of representation is that the failure lines for
the active and passive states are described differently (Statens Vegvesen 2022a).
It is also worth emphasising that the shape of the stress path is strongly influenced by the overcon-
solidation ratio (OCR) and sample quality, as shown in figure 4.12.
Figure 4.12: Influence of OCR and sample quality on the shape of the stress path (figure reproduced
from Statens Vegvesen (2022a).
In addition, an important element serving for the interpretation of the results can be emphasized by
the mobilisation charts, usually showing shear stress and pore pressure against axial strain.
37
==PAGE:50==
Chapter 4. Sampling and laboratory soil testing
Typically, one of the most important parameters needed for modelling purposes can be described by
shear strength. For the effective stress analysis, attraction and soil friction angle should be used,
which derivation from the stress path plot is shown in figure 4.11.
However, the present work focuses primarily on the interpretation of parameters for total stress
analysis, i.e. undrained shear strength cu. Its value can be found both in the stress path and in the
mobilisation shear strength diagram, as a peak value. The undrained shear strength value should be
within the range of 1-5% vertical strain. If the shear strength increases continuously with increasing
axial strain, it is advisable to select a representative value in the range of 2-5% strain, as higher
values of strength are only able to mobilise at high strains. Another possibility is to select a value at
the point where the stress path first meets the failure line (Statens Vegvesen 2022a).
Sample disturbance
As mentioned in previous sections, an important part of results evaluation consists of the determination
of sample quality. In the triaxial test, the practice is to determine the level of disturbance by monitoring
water expulsion during the consolidation phase. According to Statens Vegvesen (2022a), two criteria
are commonly used to assess sample quality, being represented by volume strain (εvol = ∆V /V0) and
void ratio change (∆e/e0) during reconsolidation to the in-situ stress state. Under ideal conditions
where no sample disturbance is encountered, a change in both parameters would not be observed.
Lunne et al. (2006) reports that void ratio criterion can be considered a better indicator of disturbance,
as it shows a more systematic change in relation to sample quality than volumetric strain. In tables
4.2 and 4.3 the criteria proposed in Statens Vegvesen (2022a) can be observed.

it is advisable to apply them carefully outside these ranges (Statens Vegvesen 2022a).
However, sample quality should be determined holistically, based on multiple observations. Statens
Vegvesen (2022a) recommends visual inspection of the sample, evaluation of the stress-strain response
in the uniaxial test, as well as assessment of the shape of stress-strain and modulus curves from
oedometer test. In triaxial test, the shape of the stress paths, stress-strain response of the material,
failure strains and soil strength can also provide evidence of sample quality (Sandven et al. 2017).

Recommendations for passive triaxial testing
An essential part of the laboratory work on this project was to conduct passive triaxial tests. These
are carried out in many geotechnical laboratories, but they are not as frequent as active tests and
can prove challenging due to a lack of experience. The following are recommendations for carrying
out passive triaxial tests (especially on the machine as shown in figure 4.10), based on the experience
gained from working on the project:
• The triaxial cell base must be attached to the frame, otherwise the two parts may lose contact
during the extension shear phase,
• The use of paper filters is not recommended - in particular, the filter on the length of the
sample can affect the result, as it has a different tensile strength than the specimen and must
be torn off when the sample fails,
• A fresh rubber membrane should be used - as it loses its elasticity over time and with the
number of uses. This is particularly important in the extension test, as this will reduce the
strength value obtained from the test,
• As mentioned earlier in this section, for the passive triaxial test, a different load cell is required
to allow the sample to be extended using suction. In this regard, there are several things to be
careful of:
– When attaching the extension top cap to the load cell, the bolt should not be screwed all
the way into the load cell - otherwise, it may result in part of the force not being detected
by the device.
– When changing the loading cell elements - from the loading button to the extension top
cap, care must be taken to ensure that the loading cell is securely fastened to the loading
rod so that water does not enter the electronics during the test.
– The surface between the extension top cap and sleeve on the top of the specimen should
be coated with silicone grease for better grip.
– The suction system vent valve should be open for at least the first hour of consolidation
to ensure contact of load cell with sample and thus allow suction.
Figure 4.13 shows a p’-q plot for a correctly performed test, while figure 4.14 shows example plots
for tests performed before the application of recommendations.
Figure 4.13: Example of a correctly performed passive triaxial test, as expected having a smooth
shape for small strains
39
==PAGE:52==
Chapter 4. Sampling and laboratory soil testing

use of filter paper, which manifests itself as amplification once the soil starts to fall and the paper
has not yet torn.

==PAGE:53==
Chapter 5
Laboratory test results
This chapter is dedicated to presenting the results of laboratory tests carried out on soil samples
taken from Tofte and Nardoveien 10 areas. It includes information on sampling, specimen handling
in the laboratory and index, oedometer and triaxial tests.
It is emphasised that the test reports from the geotechnical laboratory, which can be found in
Appendices A to F (for reasons of readability of the work) form an integral part of this chapter and
the reference to them is requested during its examination. Reports provide an interpretation of the
geotechnical parameters based on the graphs, and these parameters are overviewed in this section.
5.1
Tofte, Halsnøy
As mentioned before, Tofte is one of the main study cases in the SAUNA project conducted by
NTNU in collaboration with Statens Vegvesen, NVE, Bane NOR and Kvinnherad kommune. In 2023,
Multiconsult was engaged by NTNU to conduct geotechnical investigations and collect soil samples
for laboratory testing from the project area at Tofte. The work was carried out in November and
December 2023. The investigations were carried out in both Tofte 1 and Tofte 2 areas, but the
material for laboratory testing was extracted only from the second location. Samples were obtained
from 5 boreholes, in total 16 cylinders ∅54, of which 6 were damaged or incomplete.
5.1.1
Sampling and handling in the laboratory
The boreholes were planned by NTNU representatives (figure 5.1), but minor adjustments were made
during the course of the works in relation to the conditions encountered and the needs. The final
samples taken, which were then delivered to the geotechnical laboratory at NTNU, are described in
table 5.1, together with the depth, type of material and the date of sampling and opening.
The reasoning for using these locations is as follows (according to NTNU (2023) and discussion with
persons responsible in NTNU):
• 601 - Establishing undrained shear strength in profiles 1-1 and 3-3 (it was assessed that the
A8 and E4 would be more relevant for 1-1 (active zone), while A5 for 3-3 (passive zone)),
• 602 - Establishing undrained shear strength in profile 1-1 (passive zone),
• 606 - Establishing undrained shear strength in profile 5-5 (passive zone of ravine) - both
cylinders were damaged, therefore only index testing could be performed,
• 607 - Establishing undrained shear strength in profile 3-3 (active zone of ravine),
41
==PAGE:54==
Chapter 5. Laboratory test results
• 608 - Establishing undrained shear strength in profile 3-3 (passive zone of ravine). Samples
from the depth 30+ m for checking whether there is quick clay which could threaten the
stability of the area to the east of the ravine.
Figure 5.1: Location of the investigation points and cross-sections in the Tofte 2 area (figure
reproduced from NTNU (2023))
On the basis of the survey, a report was prepared (Multiconsult 2024b). The report contains all the
information about the task, the location of the survey points and the results of field tests performed
(total sounding, rotary pressure sounding, CPTU and pore pressure measurements). The cylinders
with the material were transported to NTNU’s geotechnical laboratory in Trondheim. Apart from
information about damage to some cylinders, there is no information about problems with the
extraction or transport of material. It is emphasised, however, that sample disturbances in layered
masses, especially with silt content, are possible.
After transport to NTNU, the cylinders were stored in a cold room belonging to the laboratory. As
can be seen in table 5.1, the samples were stored for a relatively long time, between 3 and 5 months,
which is not recommended and has a noticeable effect on sample degradation.
The samples were extracted from the cylinders using a hydraulic sample extruder and placed on
the holders. Care was taken to ensure that the sample was disturbed as little as possible, but both
extrusion and further cutting with wire saws and material handling very often resulted in minor
distortions. Particularly difficult was the handling of samples with high sensitivity and silt content.

Index testing on the samples was performed on the same day after opening, but the rest of the tests

cover it with aluminium foil for this period.
5.1.2
Description of the samples
The following observations were made during the visual examination of the samples:
• A8 (borehole 601) - grey, clay, very quick (very sensitive and liquefied when remoulded). On
the extruded sample’s surface, many small cracks and holes are visible.

• E4 (borehole 601) - grey, clay, extremely quick (very sensitive and liquefied when remoulded,
very easy to disturb the surface). Many extensive cracks around the perimeter of the extruded
sample are visible, the surface of the sample appears disturbed in many places (possibly due to
extrusion).
Figure 5.3: Sample from cylinder E4, borehole 601
• A5 (borehole 601) - grey, clay, quite quick (quite sensitive and liquefied when remoulded, but
not very much), contains small pebbles. The surface of the extruded sample is smooth.
Figure 5.4: Sample from cylinder A5, borehole 601
• C1 (borehole 602) - intense grey, clay, not so soft or very sensitive. The surface of the extruded
sample is smooth.
Figure 5.5: Sample from cylinder C1, borehole 602
• F7 (borehole 602) - dark grey with black streaks, has a strong smell, clay but feels silty. Soft
material with smaller sensitivity - when remoulded it loses strength but is sticky. Some minor

5.1.3
Index testing
In this section, the results of the index testing are presented together with a brief discussion of
samples taken from Tofte. These results, in the form of graphs and tables aiming to better illustrate
the variation of parameters with depth, are also included in the appendix A.

average density for the whole cylinder and by weighing the sample in a small ring of known volume. In

Strength parameters - fall cone and uniaxial tests
As part of index testing, two strength assessment tests of soil material were performed on samples
from Tofte: the fall cone test and the uniaxial test. As the triaxial test was considered to be
a priority, the uniaxial test was only conducted on samples from those cylinders where sufficient
material remained after dividing it into samples needed for other experiments. Stress-strain curves
and interpretation of results are included in appendix A. The fall cone test, on the other hand, was
conducted for each cylinder twice, using a sample from the top and bottom of the cylinder.
In the part of the fall cone tests, the procedure deviated slightly from the one described in section
4.3.3. For the remoulded sample, a set of 3 measurements was taken only once, instead of taking
them twice. In addition, all measurements were recorded, including those deviating more than 0.5
mm.
To determine the strength parameters and sensitivity, the smaller of the values for both undisturbed
and remoulded samples was selected when more than one set of measurements was performed. The
results obtained from both tests, together with the calculated sensitivity, are shown in table 5.4.
In many cylinders, the results of the fall cone test differ significantly between the top and bottom parts.
There is no noticeable trend that could explain why this occurs. One possibility, when comparing
the results with the conclusions of the water content values, is that the material is heavily layered
and shows different properties. Another plausible option is that the samples are of poor quality due

to the long period of storage before the cylinder was opened, particularly given that the samples
were taken from the top and bottom of the material, which may have been more exposed than the
material in the middle of its height. However, in the case of samples taken from depths >20 m, it is
very likely that the lower part of the sample is much more disturbed than the upper part, due to the
challenge in taking a sample at such a great depth. Therefore, a measurement in the upper part of
the cylinder seems to provide a more realistic value.
However, the main aim of this research was to check sensitivity and remoulded strength in order to
assess which of the collected materials show quick or brittle behaviour. As mentioned in section 4.3.3,
according to the NVE guidelines, quick clay is assumed to have a remoulded strength curfc<0.33
kPa, while brittle behaviour is assumed to result in a curfc<1.27 kPa (by performing a fall cone test
according to Eurocodes). In addition, quick clay is assumed to be characterised by a sensitivity of
St>30. If the previously described conditions are met, an areal stability analysis is required.
According to the interpreted results, only samples A8 and E4 appear to show very quick clay properties,
which is consistent with the visual inspection discussed at the beginning of the chapter. Also, materials
A5, C1 and F7 show apparent brittle behaviour and significant sensitivity, which imply the need for
an area stability analysis.
Samples A447, HS3, AL7 and T536 for some measurements meet the conditions for sensitive clay
with brittle behaviour, but it could be argued whether these results are reliable. Especially for HS3
and T536, which were excavated from depths of 24 and 46 m respectively, the measurements indicate
brittleness only in the lower part of the cylinder, where, as mentioned above, there is a risk of
significant disturbance. In addition, as noted in the previous discussion, the A447 cylinder’s bottom
part was damaged during sampling, so it is reasonable to suspect that the results in this part of the
sample will not be very reliable.
In summary, samples A8 and E4 show characteristics of quick clay material, while A5, C1 and F7
can be classified as clay with brittle behaviour. For the remaining samples, it would be advisable to
49
==PAGE:62==
Chapter 5. Laboratory test results
repeat the tests, but most of them show significant sensitivity.
5.1.4
Oedometer test
As part of this project, it was decided to perform one oedometric test for each borehole to be able to
determine whether the soil in the location is normally or over-consolidated. For this purpose, the
estimated in-situ vertical stress for the sample was compared with the pre-consolidation stress read
from the resulting graph, giving the OCR value. Information on in-situ vertical stress assumption can
be found in section 5.1.5.
Samples from the following cylinders were selected for the oedometer test: E4 (depth 11.6 m) for
borehole 601, C1 (depth 6.6 m) for borehole 602, V2 (depth 6.5 m) for borehole 607 and STJ3
(depth 8.5 m) for borehole 608. These are samples from shallow depths, where the influence of
over-consolidation would be most evident. In addition, it was decided to test sample T536 (depth 46.4
m) in borehole 608, in order to gain more information on what conditions prevail at this depth. As
both cylinders of borehole 606 were damaged, no test was carried out for them. Figures 5.14 to 5.18
show the resulting stress-strain diagrams for the tests performed, together with their interpretation.
Figure 5.14: Stress-strain curve obtained in CRS oedometer test for sample E4 (depth 11.6 m),
borehole 601

From the results, it is apparent that the soil at location 601 (in the upper part of the natural slope) is
normally consolidated, while at point 602 (lower part of the same slope) it is slightly over-consolidated.
Points 607 and 608 are located within a ravine, the origin of which is associated with past landslides
or erosion, so the ground in the specified location was expected to be over-consolidated, which was
confirmed by the test results. These show that point 607 on the top of the ravine is moderately
over-consolidated, while point 608 on the bottom of the ravine is severely over-consolidated.
An interesting result, however, is the OCR value for sample T536, which was extracted from borehole
608 similar to STJ3. In contrast to the test from 8 m depth, the one for 46 m shows characteristics
of normally consolidated soil. This may be due to the fact that the impact which caused the
over-consolidation of the shallower sample did not influence soil conditions as deep or had a small
area coverage.
5.1.5
Triaxial tests
The triaxial test was the main task in this project, providing the basis for determining the undrained
shear strength and, through compression and extension tests, allowing the actual ADP relationship
to be established. For this purpose, in-situ conditions were assessed, setting the basis to decide what
kind of triaxial tests would be most relevant for each individual sample.
Choice of triaxial test parameters
In order to obtain the most relevant results, it is important to choose the right parameters for the
triaxial test, such as consolidation stresses, earth pressure coefficient K’0 and the parameters of the
shearing phase as drained/undrained and compression/extension. Due to the nature of the material
(clay) and the objective of the task, which is to check the parameters necessary for total stress
analysis, it was presumed as a baseline that all tests would be performed as consolidated undrained.
The first step in analysing in-situ conditions was to examine which samples were taken from the
active zone and which were from the passive one. As all cylinders in borehole 606 were damaged,
they were unsuitable for triaxial testing due to the generated disturbances. All the other locations
were tested to the extent that was decided to be adequate, based on the conditions assessment,
along with the guidance provided by the project supervisors, who possess more insight into the matter
according to their knowledge acquired during site visits and contact with local authorities.
Borehole 601 is located in the upper part of the main slope, which corresponds to the active and
possibly transitional zone. It was however assessed, that the material collected from a depth of 17 m
is more relevant to the slope occurring in the direction of the ravine, which implies its passive zone.
It was therefore decided to perform both active and passive tests for all samples at this location to
evaluate ADP factors, with a K’0 value of 0.7 being chosen.
52
==PAGE:65==
Chapter 5. Laboratory test results
Point 602 is located in the lower part of the main slope, while borehole 608 is in the lower part
of the ravine. Both points are therefore in the passive zone and the main purpose of the survey
is to investigate the ADP relationship under these conditions. As two cylinders were available at
two depths for point 602, it was decided to conduct an ADP factor analysis from the shallower
one, while for the deeper one, it was agreed to survey two different K’0 to see how this affects the
results. For point 602, only a single cylinder at a depth of 8 m might have been of relevance to
slope stability, as the other two samples are well below the predicted sliding surface. For the passive
zone, coefficient K’0 was expected to be even higher than 1, so a value close to 1 was used as a
conservative assumption.
Point 607 is located in a purely active zone, therefore it was decided to perform only one active
test, based on which it would be possible to assume and confirm the correct suA value in comparison
with the results of the sounding. The same as in location 601, it was assumed that the value of K’0
representative for the active zone could be assumed as 0.7.
The values of the in-situ vertical stresses were based on simple calculations, assuming a soil weight
of approx. 19 kN/m3, and the height of the groundwater table based on piezometer measurements,
with a depth of 0 - 2 m assumed. Table 5.6 summarises the triaxial test parameters adopted for each
of the samples.

Chapter 5. Laboratory test results
Results
The full results of the triaxial tests can be found in appendix C. This section provides an overview of
the results and interpretations, with reference to the complete dataset.
Consolidation
Plots of volume change over time for the consolidation phase for each test can be found in Appendix
C. The average consolidation time ranged from 14 to 18 h and during this time the required water
expulsion rate (<5mm3 in 5 min) was achieved, as indicated by the orange dot on the graph. However,
due to visual analysis of the graph, it was decided not to establish this as the final condition for this
phase.
Based on the water expulsion measurements, a sample quality analysis was carried out, which is
shown on page 6 for each test in Appendix C. It can be seen that for many tests the sample quality is
evaluated as poor, which may indicate that they have been disturbed during sampling, transportation
or preparation in the laboratory. Of particular note may be the fact that the samples were tested 3 to
even 6 months after sampling, which, as previously mentioned in section 4.2, has a major impact on
sensitive clay in particular. In addition, it is remarked that the results of the sample quality analysis
can be affected by the procedure used, such as the use of saturated filters and flushing prior to the
procedure, which provides additional water to the system.
B-value
According to the B-check procedure discussed in section 4.5, the B-value should reach 0.95. If not,
the saturation phase should be repeated and the B-value checked again. B-check graphs can be
found on sheet 5 for each of the tests in Appendix C. As can be seen, for none of the samples
the value reached the required value, being in the 0.72 - 0.9 range. Based on discussions with the
supervisor, it was decided not to perform a re-saturation. It was concluded that this situation should
not have a major impact on the value of the undrained shear strength, as this mainly affects the
shape of the graph in the mobilisation phase, while it is not an important element for this project.
The limited time available for testing and the large number of samples were also a factor. However,
the author notes the validity of carrying out repeat saturation in such a situation, using, for example,
a back pressure of 700 kPa (as in Multiconsult (2024a)).
Shearing phase
Shearing of the sample was in all cases carried out under undrained conditions, except from one
sample (denoted as A5_tx3). Both compression and extension tests were performed to determine
ADP factors. All triaxial test reports, along with the stress paths (NTH/NTNU, q-p’ and MIT
versions) and the shear strength mobilisation graph, can be found in Appendix C. Figure 5.19
summarises the curves of all undrained tests in the q-p’ plane.
As can be seen, the failure line (indicated by the dotted line) for both active and passive states can
be determined from the tests performed. The tests converge well, despite slight differences that may
be due to disturbance, test conditions, deviations in setting the readings to zero before initiating the
tests and also deviations in the procedure, which are described for each case in Appendix C.

==PAGE:67==
Chapter 5. Laboratory test results
Figure 5.19: Overview of the results in p’-q plane from triaxial tests on samples from Tofte
An interesting relationship that can be observed in the graph, which was previously described in
section 4.5 (Figure 4.12) is the shape of the curve in relation to the OCR values. Samples from
borehole 601 (A8, E4, A5) were subjected to normal consolidation, emphasised by the rounded
curve path, while a stiffer response is observed for boreholes 607 and 608 (V2 and STJ3), where
the ground was significantly over-consolidated. Samples from 602 (C1 and F7), which are slightly
over-consolidated (OCR = 2.4) have intermediate characteristics.
Table 5.7 shows a summary of the undrained shear strength (su) values read from the graphs as a
peak amplitude, where the observed value was in the range for axial strain up to 5%. Where a higher
shear strength was mobilised for higher strains, according to the information in section 4.5, it was
decided to select a value representative of axial strain in the range of 2 to 5% (preferably for when
the stress path first approaches failure line).
In table 5.7 AP factor was determined from the undrained shear strength readings, while the AD value
was taken as the average between 1 and AP factor. It can be seen that the values are significantly
higher than those recommended by NIFS (2014a), i.e. suP/suA = 0.35 and suD/suA = 0.63. No
apparent correlations were noted between the ADP factors and other assigned values, such as water
content, density or sensitivity, but it should be noted that the range of tests is very small and it would
be difficult to draw general conclusions about such correlations. However, all AP values are above
0.4, mostly fluctuating around the value of 0.5. This supports the idea that the values for ADP

5.2
Nardoveien 10, Trondheim
The Nardoveien case is described in section 3.2. The soil sampling was carried out in January 2024
and 2 of the cylinders ∅75 from borehole 205 (cylinders no. 102 and 104, borehole shown in
figure 5.20) were transferred to the geotechnical laboratory at NTNU, with the intention of carrying
out investigations, in particular triaxial testing. The plan was to perform standard index testing
(Appendix D), 1 oedometer test (Appendix E) and 1 triaxial compression test and 3 extension tests,
using different values of coefficient K’0 (0.6, 0.8 and 1.0). Unfortunately, after performing the
tests, it became apparent that the triaxial machine was not adequately prepared for the passive
tests, which affected the results and made analysis impossible (see section 4.5). It was decided to
drill an additional borehole in a similar location and sample EAT36 was extracted from a depth of
9.00-9.80m. This sampling was carried out on 16.04.2024 by the NTNU geotechnical laboratory with
the approval of Olav Olsen AS and the project contractor. The sample was opened on 06.05.2024,
which is approx. 3 weeks later. A water content and fall cone test, included in Appendix D, and 5
triaxial tests, presented in Appendix F, were conducted on the specimen.

site (figure reproduced from Olav Olsen AS (2024a))
Tests on samples from Nardoveien 10 were carried out in a manner analogous to that of Tofte
described in the previous section, so here only the results with interpretation will be presented.

5.2.4
Triaxial test
As mentioned above, 5 triaxial tests were carried out on material from EAT36 sample. As the material
was only taken from one location, it was decided to carry out both active and passive tests with

stress states and relate the results to different stress zones (passive and active), as the coefficient

while the full set of results is presented in Appendix F.

same principle as in the previous section for Tofte, and summarised in table 5.9.

As is clearly noticeable, the ADP factors for K’0 equal to 0.6 and 1.0 differ considerably, with the
former not even reaching the recommended value. However, as predicted and confirmed by Olav
Olsen AS, it is anticipated that for the location of the borehole (i.e. the lower part of the slope and
the passive zone), the K’0 may even exceed 1.0, so assuming a value of 1.0 would be a conservative
assumption. Under these circumstances, an appropriate ADP value for this location would be the
one for K’0 = 1.0, which is again significantly higher than recommended.
However, it is worth noting that the ADP value is intended to compare undrained shear strength in
the active and passive zones. It is expected that in the active zone, K’0 may be close to 0.6, so a
representative value for the active zone would be suA = 35.6 kPa, while for the passive zone, based
on previous considerations, suP = 18.6 kPa. It therefore follows that for the analysis of slope stability,
an AP factor of 0.52 can be assumed.
In addition, it is noteworthy that for various values of coefficient K’0, the ADP factors obtained
vary considerably. This indicates that these factors cannot be directly translated into different stress
states in the ground, as this can result in a significant error.
61
==PAGE:74==
Chapter 6
Discussion
This chapter presents a discussion on the selection of ADP parameters based on laboratory tests
and their application in computational analysis. As part of this chapter, a numerical analysis of
slope stability in two cross sections in Tofte in PLAXIS software using the NGI-ADP soil model is
presented.
6.1
Selection of ADP factors for stability analysis
The choice of appropriate soil modelling parameters is crucial in the analysis and design process. In
particular, the use of FEM software can provide a very close approximation to the actual soil response,
allowing us to avoid the conservative assumptions used in simplified calculations. Chapter 2 addresses
the issue of sensitive clay anisotropy and describes the proposed NGI-ADP soil model for undrained
analysis. It uses as its main input parameters the active undrained shear strength taken directly from
soil tests (e.g. CPTU or triaxial test) and ADP factors which describe the relationship between shear
strength in the active, passive and transition stress zones. This makes it suitable for engineering
applications where su values obtained from geotechnical investigations can be introduced as direct
input to the modelling. As described in section 2.6, in current practice in Norway ADP factors are
adopted based on the recommendations introduced in NIFS (2014a), which specify suP/suA = 0.35
and suD/suA = 0.63.
As mentioned in chapter 1, the recommended ADP parameters for sensitive and quick clays are
expected to be underestimated for slopes. These values have been chosen on the basis of a number
of soil tests, but the emphasis has been on flat areas, as noted in section 2.6. As described in section
2.5, in a slope the principal stress pattern is not based on the vertical and horizontal directions as in
horizontal terrain. This means that there is a non-zero shear stress in the soil, which is an additional
factor influencing the state of anisotropy in the soil. For this reason, the difference in shear strength
between the active and passive zones is expected to be less in incilined terrain than in flat terrain.
The present study, based on an analysis of two study cases, Tofte and Nardoveien 10, showed that the
ADP parameters obtained from laboratory tests were significantly higher than those recommended.
The parameters obtained directly from the laboratory tests showed that all values of the AP factor
(suP/suA) were greater than 0.4, with most reaching values close to 0.5. However, the aim of this
work is not to propose new recommended values for ADP factors for slopes. Rather, it is to propose
a different approach to the adoption of input parameters for soil models based on clay anisotropy in
the computational analysis process. The aim is to assess the effect on stability analysis results of
adopting actual ADP factors obtained from laboratory tests rather than the recommended values.
ADP factors from laboratory tests are evaluated by comparing active and passive undrained shear
strengths obtained from triaxial tests. However, the recommended ADP values are often used for
slope analysis, as the example from Multiconsult (2018) shows. In this case, despite laboratory
62
==PAGE:75==
Chapter 6. Discussion
testing and interpretation of ADP values, the recommended values were still used.
Based on samples taken at Tofte and Nardoveien 10, it was possible to determine ADP parameters for
site stability analysis at both sites, although the approach was different. At Tofte, several boreholes
were completed at different locations representative of different stress zones. Based on the assessed
in-situ conditions, appropriate input parameters were selected for each point, in particular the earth
pressure coefficient K’0. This made it possible to obtain an overview of the characteristics for both
active and passive conditions (the results are shown in table 5.7). At Nardoveien 10, on the other
hand, samples were taken from only one borehole, so in order to represent the change in conditions
along the length of the slope, it was decided to carry out a triaxial active and passive test with
different values of the K’0 factor. For the research purposes of this project, it was decided to perform
a series of tests with different K’0 values. In the case of the geotechnical testing of the site by
Multiconsult (2024a), K’0 = 0.8 was chosen for the active test and K’0 ≈1 for the passive test. As
described in section 5.2, the in-situ stress conditions in the form of K’0 adopted for testing have a
significant impact on the results of the ADP factors. While it may seem reasonable to use a single
K’0 value that is representative of the site, research has shown that using different K’0 values for
active and passive tests, representative of each zone, can give more favourable results in terms of
ADP factors. Assuming that the layer of material is present along the entire length of the slope
and that only the stress distribution changes, this can be considered a reasonable assumption. On
the other hand, the assumption of a higher degree of anisotropy than in-situ conditions should be
avoided, as this can harm the quality of the sample and the resulting shear strength values if applied
during the consolidation phase.
In order to test the difference between the two approaches of selecting ADP factors, using the
recommended values and the values obtained in laboratory tests, a stability analysis was conducted
on two cross-sections at Tofte. One is a cross-section through the main slope, and the other is a
cross-section through a ravine, i.e. formation that is most likely the result of a landslide that occurred
in the past. Thus, it was observed how the proposed approach gives different results for natural
slopes and slopes resulting from geological processes such as landslides. The analysis performed and
the conclusions are described in the next section.
6.2
Numerical modelling of slope stability using the NGI-
ADP model
As already mentioned, to compare the values obtained from the two discussed approaches for adopting
ADP values, a stability analysis of two sections from Tofte was carried out. For this purpose, the
models developed as part of Linga (2024), shared by the author, were used.
Both models were developed in the software PLAXIS. All layers with clay characteristics were modelled
using the NGI-ADP soil model in undrained conditions, while the other layers were represented using
the Mohr-Coulomb model. It is indicated that, concerning the purpose of this project, it was not
deemed necessary to focus on determining the undrained shear strength profile at depth, the sole
purpose being to check the effect on the results of changing the assumed ADP parameters. Therefore,
the undrained shear strength profile and the other soil parameters (except ADP factors), geometry,
water conditions and boundary conditions remain unchanged from the models presented in Linga
(2024) (the only deviation is described later in this section). The analysis of both cross-sections as
well as the results and conclusions are described in the following section.
Cross-section of Tofte NTNU1 (1-1 in figure 5.1)
The NTNU1 cross-section (1-1 in figure 5.1) describes the situation on a natural slope descending
towards the bay. It is the slope on which two boreholes have been drilled and tested in the laboratory
63
==PAGE:76==
Chapter 6. Discussion
for ADP factors, namely 601 in the active zone and 602 in the passive zone (see figure 5.1). Quick
clay was detected at 601 and highly sensitive clay with low remoulded shear strength was identified
at 602.
The model used for the stability analysis is shown in figure 6.1. The slope has been modelled from
the point of flattening of the terrain at the top to the location of the transition under the water level.
As can be seen, in the upper part of the escarpment it was assumed that the bedrock level reaches
almost to the ground level. In addition, 3 different layers of sensitive clay were identified - silty clay,
quick clay and soft clay. The layer marked in red is dry crust clay, modelled using the Mohr-Coulomb
model.
Figure 6.1: Numerical model in PLAXIS for cross-section NTNU1 with adopted ADP factors from
laboratory testing
Based on the result data obtained from the triaxial tests shown in table 5.7, it was decided to make
the following assumptions for the following layers regarding ADP factors:
• Silty clay (siltig leire), a layer which is located at shallow depth and mainly in the upper part
of the slope, was found to be representative by a sample from borehole 601, marked A8 (from
7 m depth), providing AP/DP values of 0.44/0.72.
• Quick clay (kvikkleire), thick continuous layer in both active and passive zones. The value
for sample E4 from borehole 601 (depth 11 m) was selected as it exhibits very quick clay
characteristics. Moreover, at the foot of the slope, in the passive zone, the layer is located at
a relatively shallow depth, which supports this choice instead of the parameters for the sample
from a depth of 17 m (A5). AP/DP values were assumed as 0.54/0.77.
• Soft clay (bløt leire), a layer occurring in the passive zone, sample C1 from borehole 602 (6
m depth) was considered as representative. In this case, it could be argued that a value for
sample F7 (from 15 m depth), which was described as soft and is at a more appropriate depth,
would be more appropriate, but in this case, no active test was carried out to obtain ADP
factors. AP/DP values were adopted as 0.51/0.76.
The assumed ADP factors for each layer are shown in figure 6.1.
Two analyses were performed on the model, one with the recommended AP/AD values of 0.35/0.63
and then with the values mentioned above. The other values and geometry remained unchanged.
The calculation involved two stages, the equilibrium stage and a safety analysis (using the ’phi/c
reduction method’), which determined the factor of safety. Figure 6.2 provides the total displacement
graph, indicating the gliding surface, for calculations using the recommended ADP factors, while
figure 6.3 is the corresponding graph for calculations using values based on laboratory testing. The
figures show the corresponding factor of safety values obtained (indicated as Ftot).
64
==PAGE:77==
Chapter 6. Discussion
Figure 6.2: The total displacement graph for safety analysis using recommended ADP values in
cross-section NTNU1, with information about obtained factor of safety
Figure 6.3: The total displacement graph for safety analysis using ADP values assessed from laboratory
testing in cross-section NTNU1, with information about obtained factor of safety
The resulting factor of safety values are:
• F = 1.17 for the recommended ADP factors,
• F = 1.35 for ADP factors values obtained from laboratory tests.
This indicates that using the proposed approach, there is an increase in the factor of safety of
approximately 15% compared to the values obtained from the analysis using the recommended ADP
factors. This is a significant increase, particularly in the case of results which lie on the verge of
requirements in NVE (2020). It is worth mentioning that in this case, despite the improvement in the
result, the value of F = 1.4, which is requested for measure category K3 where there is a potential
deterioration of the stability conditions in the area, was not achieved.
65
==PAGE:78==
Chapter 6. Discussion
Cross-section of Tofte NTNU5 (3-3 in figure 5.1)
As mentioned earlier, the second cross-section analysed is located within a ravine, which was
presumably formed by a landslide and the resulting deposition of material at the foot of the slope.
This assumption is confirmed by the result of the oedometer test at point 608, which indicates a
significant over-consolidation of the upper soil layer. Three boreholes were completed in this section -
607 in the active zone, at the top of the ravine, 608 in the passive zone, at the very foot of the slope
and the previously described borehole 601, located some distance from the ravine, with representative
sample A5 from a depth of 17 m. A sample from borehole 601 indicates sensitive clay with relatively
low remoulded shear strength, but the others were identified as sensitive clay but of relatively high
remoulded shear strength, not within the range indicating the need for a stability analysis according
to NVE (2020).
The model used to analyse the stability of the section described is shown in figure 6.4. Two clay
layers modelled by the NGI-ADP model - quick clay and silty clay - are distinguished. The model
covers the top of the ravine, which is flat, and the slope itself. Due to the possibility of specifying an
inclination of the terrain within the soil layer, a division of the layers between the flat part and the
sloping part is used, but apart from the slope, all other parameters are the same for both parts. In
addition to the mentioned materials, there are two layers modelled with the Mohr-Coulomb model -
dry crust clay (as in the previous section, marked in red), and sandy material, marked in yellow.
Figure 6.4: Numerical model in PLAXIS for cross-section NTNU5 with adopted ADP factors from
laboratory testing
In the equilibrium phase, using the recommended ADP factors, an error was returned, warning of
instability detected in the numerical model. For this reason, all values describing the su profile were
increased by 10%, so that the model was stable in the initial phase and could be used for further
analysis.
The following ADP factors were adopted from laboratory geotechnical tests:
• Quick clay (kvikkleire), parameters based on a sample from borehole 608, marked STJ3 (8 m
depth), giving AP/AD values of 0.5/0.75,
• Silty clay (siltig leire), parameters based on a deeper sample from borehole 601, numbered A5
(depth 17 m), chosen due to the depth of the modelled material.
In this case, the names of the layers do not coincide with the characteristics of the samples collected,
but it was decided to use the depth of the samples as the main criterion, especially as only ADP
66
==PAGE:79==
Chapter 6. Discussion
factors for two samples were obtained at this cross-section. This appears to be a reasonable approach
given the point-wise nature of the samples collected and the general tendency of clay materials to
achieve high ADP values. Figure 6.4 shows the adopted ADP factors based on laboratory tests.
For cross-section NTNU5 an analogous analysis was carried out as for the NTNU1. In figures 6.5
and 6.6, respectively, the results of the analysis based on the recommended and laboratory values of
the ADP factors can be found.
Figure 6.5: The total displacement graph for safety analysis using recommended ADP values in
cross-section NTNU5, with information about obtained factor of safety
Figure 6.6: The total displacement graph for safety analysis using ADP values assessed from laboratory
testing in cross-section NTNU5, with information about obtained factor of safety
The resulting factor of safety values are:
• F = 1.01 for the recommended ADP factors,
• F = 1.09 for ADP factors obtained from laboratory tests.
67
==PAGE:80==
Chapter 6. Discussion
It can be observed that the difference between the results is much smaller than for the cross-section
of a natural slope, without the influence of geological processes such as a previous landslide. In this
case, the difference between the results is only about 8% and the requirements imposed by NVE
(2020) are not met for both cases.
Nardoveien 10, Trondheim
Due to time constraints, a numerical analysis was not conducted for the section at Nardoveien 10,
Trondheim. However, based on the data from the stability analysis described in Olav Olsen AS
(2024c), it was decided to analyse the effects of the ADP factors from the laboratory analysis, in
compliance with earlier findings, concerning the requirements of NVE (2020).
According to the Olav Olsen AS (2024c), the factor of safety for today’s situation in cross-section
E-E (fig. 5.20) for the undrained analysis is 1.28. The NGI-ADP soil model for clay was adopted in
the analysis with the ADP values assumed as recommended values, even though studies Multiconsult
(2024a) have shown that the ratio could have been chosen as higher.
As already mentioned in chapter 3, the adopted measurement category is K4, while in Olav Olsen
AS (2024c) it is assumed that the hazard level can be assessed as medium. In this case, NVE (2020)
requires that, in the event of a deterioration of the conditions during construction, a factor of safety
of at least 1.4*fs must always be maintained (in this case this gives 1.61 according to Olav Olsen AS
(2024c)) or, in the event of no degradation of the situation at any time, the initial factor must be
at least 1.4. A lower value requires an improvement of the conditions in the form of appropriate
protective measures or safeguards (in this case Olav Olsen AS (2024c) states that the situation
should be improved by 3.5%). Based on the tests carried out as described in section 5.2, a suP/suA
value of 0.52 can be assumed.
Assuming that an improvement in conditions of about 15% can be expected, comparable to the
Tofte analysis (which, however, should be supported by numerical analysis), it can expected that
with the use of ADP factors based on laboratory tests the factor of safety will increase to about
1.47. Thus, according to the requirements of NVE (2020), it would not be necessary to improve
the conditions during the construction process, but only to maintain the existing state. This would
imply a real limitation in the application of the proposed measurements. However, it is worth noting
that the maintenance of stability conditions requires the application of appropriate measurements,
in this case, a backfill coordinated with the demolition works, and therefore it would need to be
assessed how much of a difference this change would make in terms of the planned works. Based
on the results obtained, it would certainly not be possible to omit the application of the designed
measurements in their entirety.
6.3
Comments on the limitations of the analysis performed
The analysis carried out provides some information in response to the questions posed in chapter 1.
However, it is worth noting some limitations, arising mainly due to the restricted time and scope
of the project. First of all, it has to be taken into account that the analysis presented is based on
two study cases only, and therefore it is not advisable to draw far-reaching conclusions for the whole
complex topic. In addition, it is worth noting that some of the laboratory tests carried out, providing
input parameters, indicate low sample quality. As discussed in chapter 5, this may be partially due
to the limitations of current methods of assessing sample disturbance, but an important factor is
also the lack of previous experience of the author of this project in laboratory testing and the time
constraint for this work. For this reason, there were deviations from the standard in the geotechnical
investigation procedure, as described in chapter 5 and the corresponding Appendices.
68
==PAGE:81==
Chapter 6. Discussion
In addition, there are factors beyond the author’s ability to predict and control, such as disturbances
resulting from sampling, transportation and storage of samples, which are described in sections 4 and
5. Another limitation, which was mentioned earlier, is that the numerical models do not describe
very accurately the shear strength conditions obtained from the field results, but in this case, it was
considered sufficient for the purpose of this work to check the effect of assuming different values of
ADP factors only.
However, based on the convergence of the results obtained, the consistency with theory and the
discussion with the supervisors, it was decided that it is possible to give a recommendation based
on the results of this project, which can be one of the contributions to the discussion on the topic
addressed in this thesis.
69
==PAGE:82==
Chapter 7
Conclusions
This chapter summarises this report and presents possible directions for further work on the topic.
The summary briefly describes the outline and purpose of the project, the conducted research and
recommendations based on the results obtained. Conclusions are intended to provide a brief overview
of the topic described in the given report.
7.1
Summary and recommendations
This project focused on the study of ADP factors for natural slopes in quick clays based on laboratory
testing. The hypothesis behind it is that due to the deviation of principal stresses from the vertical
and horizontal directions for inclined terrain, the ADP situation for slopes differs significantly from the
situation in flat terrain and therefore the recommended values in NIFS (2014a) are underestimated
for slopes.
An analysis of ADP factors was conducted on the basis of laboratory tests carried out for two study
cases from Norway - Tofte, Halsnoy and Nardoveien 10, Trondheim. Index testing was carried out on
the samples to determine soil type, ground conditions and to estimate mechanical parameters, an
oedometer test to check the degree of consolidation and triaxial testing, both active and passive, to
assess undrained shear strength and ADP factors.
As expected, all ADP factors obtained were well above the recommended values. To investigate the
effect of changing ADP factors in the stability analysis, undrained numerical stability analysis were
performed on the Tofte slope in two cross-sections, using the NGI-ADP soil model.
Based on the findings presented in this project, it was decided to make the following recommendations
and comments:
• As expected, ADP factors for sensitive and quick clays in natural slopes obtained from laboratory
tests (and thus reflecting actual conditions) are significantly higher than the values recommended
by NIFS (2014a) (suP/suA = 0.35 and suD/suA = 0.63). Based on the research performed, it
can be concluded that suP/suA values of 0.4 to 0.5 can be expected from laboratory tests. In
the absence of a direct shear test it is suggested to consider the suD/suA value as the average
value between 1 and the obtained suP/suA.
• For natural slopes, there are grounds for conducting laboratory tests for the determination of
ADP factors when the results of the analysis on the basis of the recommended values give
results slightly below the requirements. Based on the studies undertaken, it can be concluded
that the improvement in conditions using the results of the laboratory tests may be as high as
approx. 15% compared to the use of the recommended values. If the results of the stability
analysis are significantly below the requirements, there can generally be no justification for
conducting a laboratory analysis.
70
==PAGE:83==
Chapter 7. Conclusions
• In the case of landforms resulting from geological activities such as landslides and the associated
deposition of material, it is anticipated based on the studies performed, that the impact of
adopting higher ADP factors will not be as significant as in the case of natural slopes. In
the case presented in this study, this was an improvement of approx. 8 %. It is therefore
recommended in a similar case to analyse the effect of altering the ADP parameters on the
change in the factor of safety before a decision to conduct additional studies.
• It is emphasised that when carrying out tests to determine ADP factors, it is important to
adequately assess in-situ stress conditions, especially the K’0 coefficient. When testing multiple
locations, a K’0 value representative of the individual point should be used. However, if testing
is carried out at a single location, in order to obtain factors representative of both the active
and passive zones at the same time, it is reasonable to carry out the active test with a K’0 value
representative of the active stress zone and the passive test with a K’0 value representative
of the passive zone. The study shows that it enables to obtain more favourable results with
respect to ADP factors. If non-representative values are used in both tests, it is possible to
obtain significantly lower results, therefore this should be avoided.
7.2
Possible further work
As described in chapter 6, the biggest limitation of this project is the fact of the limited scope and
the time constraints. Therefore, if this topic is to be developed further, it would be advisable to
perform more tests on samples from a larger number of sites, so that they can provide an adequate
response to the data collected in NIFS (2014a). On this basis, it would be advisable to undertake a
more advanced stability analysis in FEM, adopting an undrained shear strength profile based on the
parameters obtained, as well as comparing the results with those of an effective stress analysis.
Another aspect is the possibility of investigating the more fundamental principles of the effect of
the terrain inclination on the ADP situation. As part of the discussions with the supervisors, the
possibility of taking soil samples in the direction of principal stresses in the slope, and therefore
deviating from the vertical direction, was indicated. In this way, it would be possible to test samples in
the direction of principal stresses, which could provide information on the general governing principles
of the anisotropic situation in slopes.
